10094-8{_}1361{_}"May I ask your Excellency's permission to appoint Mr. Stewart here one of my ensigns?"
10094-8{_}1362{_}"Certainly," said the governor heartily.
10094-8{_}1363{_}"From what I have seen of Mr. Stewart, I should conclude that nothing could be better;" and when I tried to stammer my thanks, he waved his hand to me kindly and rang for wine.
10094-8{_}1364{_}"Let us drink," he said, as he filled the glasses, "to the success of our arms and the establishment of his Majesty's dominion on the Ohio."
10094-8{_}1365{_}CHAPTER IX  MY FIRST TASTE OF WARFARE   Whatever defects Dinwiddie may have had, indecision was certainly not one of them, and the very next day the machinery was set in motion for the advance against the French.
10094-8{_}1366{_}Colonel Joshua Fry was selected to head the expedition, and Colonel Washington made second in command.
10094-8{_}1367{_}Colonel Fry at one time taught mathematics at William and Mary, but found the routine of the class-room too humdrum, and so sought a more exciting life.
10094-8{_}1368{_}He had found it along the borders of the frontier, and in 1750 was made colonel of militia and member of the governor's council.
10094-8{_}1369{_}Two years later, he was sent to Logstown to treat with the Indians, and made a map of the colony.
10094-8{_}1370{_}He knew the frontier as well as any white man, and because of this was chosen our commander.
10094-8{_}1371{_}Not a moment was to be lost, for Colonel Washington, while at Fort le Boeuf, had observed the great preparations made by the French to descend the Allegheny in the spring and take possession of the Ohio valley, but we hoped to forestall them.
10094-8{_}1372{_}The triangle between the forks of the Ohio was admirably adapted for fortification, and it was proposed to throw up a fort there so that the French would get a warm reception when their canoes came floating down the river, and be forced to retreat to the Lakes.
10094-8{_}1373{_}Dinwiddie's energy was wide-felt, and the whole colony was soon astir.
10094-8{_}1374{_}He convened the House of Burgesses, laid Colonel Washington's report before it, and secured a grant of 10,000 for purposes of defense; he urged the governors of the other colonies, from the Carolinas north to Jersey, to send reinforcements at once to Will's Creek, whence the start was to be made; he sent messengers with presents to the Ohio Indians, pressing them to take up the hatchet against the French, and authorized the enlistment of three hundred men.
10094-8{_}1375{_}William Trent, an Indian trader, and brother-in-law of Colonel George Croghan, was commissioned to raise a company of a hundred men from among the backwoodsmen along the frontier, and started at once for the Ohio country to get his men together and begin work on the fort, the main body to follow so soon as it could be properly equipped.
10094-8{_}1376{_}Long before this I had secured my uniform and accoutrements,--which my three shillings a day were far from paying for,--and was kept busy superintending the storage of wagons or drilling under Captain Adam Stephen, in whose company I was, at Alexandria.
10094-8{_}1377{_}The men were for the most part poor whites, who had enlisted because they could earn their bread no other way, and promised to make but indifferent soldiers.
10094-8{_}1378{_}We were provided with ten cannon, all four-pounders, which had been presented by the king to Virginia, and eighty barrels of powder, together with small-arms, thirty tents, and six months' provision of flour, pork, and beef.
10094-8{_}1379{_}These were forwarded to Will's Creek as rapidly as possible, but at the best it was slow work, and April was in sight before the expedition was ready to move.
10094-8{_}1380{_}During near all of this time, Colonel Washington was virtually in command, for Colonel Fry was taken with a fever, which kept him for the most part to his bed.
10094-8{_}1381{_}There seemed no prospect of his improvement, so he ordered the expedition to advance without him, he to follow so soon as he could sit a horse.
10094-8{_}1382{_}That time was never to come, for he died at Will's Creek on the last day of May.
10094-8{_}1383{_}So at last the advance commenced, and from daylight to sunset we fought our way through the forest.
10094-8{_}1384{_}It rained almost incessantly, and I admit the work was more severe than I had ever done, for the bridle-paths were too narrow to permit the passage of the guns and wagons, and a way had to be cut for them; yet all the men were in good spirits, animated by the example of Colonel Washington and the other officers.
10094-8{_}1385{_}Those I came to know best were of Captain Stephen's company, and a braver, merrier set of men it has never been my privilege to meet.
10094-8{_}1386{_}We were drawn from all the quarters of the globe.
10094-8{_}1387{_}There was Lieutenant William Poison, a Scot, who had been concerned in the rebellion of '45, and so found it imperative to come to Virginia to spend the remainder of his days, though at the first scent of battle he was in arms again.
10094-8{_}1388{_}There was Ensign William, Chevalier de Peyronie, a French Protestant, driven from his home much as the Fontaine family, and who had settled in Virginia.
10094-8{_}1389{_}There was Lieutenant Thomas Waggoner, whom I was to know so well a year later.
10094-8{_}1390{_}And above all, there was Ensign Carolus Gustavus de Spiltdorph, a quiet, unassuming fellow, but brave as a lion, who lies to-day in an unmarked grave on the bank of the Monongahela.
10094-8{_}1391{_}I can see him yet, with his blue eyes and blond beard, sitting behind a cloud of smoke in one corner of the tent, listening to our wild talk with a queer gleam in his eyes, and putting in a word of dry sarcasm now and then.
10094-8{_}1392{_}For when the day's march was done, those of us who were not on duty gathered in our tent and talked of the time when we should meet the French.
10094-8{_}1393{_}And Peyronie, because, though a Frenchman, he had suffered most at their hands, was the most bloodthirsty of us all.
10094-8{_}1394{_}Then the first blow fell.
10094-8{_}1395{_}It was the night of the twentieth of April, and our force had halted near Colonel Cresap's house, sixteen miles from Will's Creek.
10094-8{_}1396{_}I was in charge of the sentries to the west of the camp.
10094-8{_}1397{_}The weather had been cold and threatening, with a dash of rain now and then, and we had made only five miles that day, the guns and wagons miring in the muddy road, which for the most part was through a marsh.
10094-8{_}1398{_}As evening came, the rain had set in steadily, and the sentries protected themselves as best they could behind the trees or under hastily constructed shelters.
10094-8{_}1399{_}I had just made my first round and found all well, when I heard a sentry near by challenge sharply.
10094-8{_}1400{_}"What is it?"
10094-8{_}1401{_}I cried, hastening to him, and then I saw that he had stopped a horseman.
10094-8{_}1402{_}The horse was breathing in short, uncertain gasps, as though near winded.
10094-8{_}1403{_}"A courier from the Ohio, so he says, sir," answered the sentry.
10094-8{_}1404{_}"With an urgent message for Colonel Washington," added the man on horseback.
10094-8{_}1405{_}"Very well," I said, "come with me," and catching the horse by the bridle, I started toward the commander's tent, in which a light was still burning.
10094-8{_}1406{_}A word to the sentry before it brought Colonel Washington himself to the door, and he signed for us to enter.
10094-8{_}1407{_}The courier slipped from his horse, and would have fallen, had I not caught him and placed him on his feet.
10094-8{_}1408{_}"'T is the first time I have left the saddle for two days," he gasped, and I helped him into the tent, where he dropped upon a stool.
10094-8{_}1409{_}Washington poured out a glass of brandy and handed it to him.
10094-8{_}1410{_}He swallowed it at a gulp, and it gave him back a little of his strength.
10094-8{_}1411{_}"I bring bad news, Colonel Washington," he said.
10094-8{_}1412{_}"Lieutenant Ward and his whole command were captured by the French on the seventeenth, and the fort at the forks of the Ohio is in their hands."
10094-8{_}1413{_}I turned cold under the blow, but Washington did not move a muscle, only his mouth seemed to tighten at the corners.
10094-8{_}1414{_}"How did it happen?"
10094-8{_}1415{_}he asked.
10094-8{_}1416{_}"Captain Trent and his men arrived at the Ohio on the tenth of April," said the courier, "and we set to work at once to throw up the fort.
10094-8{_}1417{_}We made good progress, but on the morning of the seventeenth, while Captain Trent and thirty of the men were absent, leaving Lieutenant Ward in command, the river was suddenly covered with canoes crowded with French and Indians.
10094-8{_}1418{_}There were at least eight hundred of them, and they had a dozen pieces of artillery.
10094-8{_}1419{_}We had no choice but to surrender."
10094-8{_}1420{_}"On what terms?"
10094-8{_}1421{_}questioned Washington quickly.
10094-8{_}1422{_}"That we march out with the honors of war and return to Virginia."
10094-8{_}1423{_}"And this was done?"
10094-8{_}1424{_}"Yes, this was done.
10094-8{_}1425{_}Lieutenant Ward and his men will join you in a day or two."
10094-8{_}1426{_}"You have done well," said Washington warmly.
10094-8{_}1427{_}"I am sure Lieutenant Ward could have done naught else under the circumstances.
10094-8{_}1428{_}Forty men are not expected to resist eight hundred, and I shall see that the occurrence is properly represented to the governor.
10094-8{_}1429{_}Lieutenant Stewart, will you see that a meal and a good bed be provided?
10094-8{_}1430{_}Good night, gentlemen."
10094-8{_}1431{_}We saluted and left the tent, and I led him over to our company quarters, where the best we had was placed before him.
10094-8{_}1432{_}Other officers, who had got wind of his arrival, dropped in, and he told again the story of the meeting with the enemy.
10094-8{_}1433{_}It was certain that there were from six to eight hundred French and a great number of Indians before us, while we were barely three hundred, and as I returned to my post, I wondered if Colonel Washington would dare press on to face such odds.
10094-8{_}1434{_}The answer came in the morning, when the order was given to march as usual.
10094-8{_}1435{_}Two days later, we had reached Will's Creek, where we found Lieutenant Ward and his men awaiting us.
10094-8{_}1436{_}He stated that there were not less than a thousand French at the forks of the Ohio.
10094-8{_}1437{_}It was sheer folly to advance with our petty force in face of odds so overwhelming, and a council of the officers was called by Colonel Washington to determine what course to follow.
10094-8{_}1438{_}It was decided that we advance as far as Red Stone Creek, on the Monongahela, thirty-seven miles this side the Forks, and there erect a fortification and await fresh orders.
10094-8{_}1439{_}Stores had already been built at Red Stone for our munitions, and from there our great guns could be sent by water so soon as we were ready to attack the French.
10094-8{_}1440{_}In conclusion, it was judged that it were better to occupy our men in cutting a road through the wilderness than that they should be allowed to waste their time in idleness and dissipation.
10094-8{_}1441{_}Captain Trent and the thirty men who were with him, hearing from the Indians of the disaster which had overtaken their companions, marched back to meet us, and joined us the next day.
10094-8{_}1442{_}Trent himself met cold welcome, for his absence from the fort at the time of the attack was held to be most culpable.
10094-8{_}1443{_}Dinwiddie was so enraged, when he learned of it, that he ordered Trent court-martialed forthwith, but this was never done.
10094-8{_}1444{_}His backwoodsmen were wild and reckless fellows, incapable of discipline, and soon took themselves off to the settlements, while we toiled on westward through the now unbroken forest.
10094-8{_}1445{_}Our advance to Will's Creek had been difficult enough, but it was nothing to the task which now confronted us, for the country grew more rough and broken, and there was not the semblance of a road.
10094-8{_}1446{_}We were a week in making twenty miles, and accomplished that only by labor well-nigh superhuman.
10094-8{_}1447{_}The story of one day was the story of all the others.
10094-8{_}1448{_}Obstacles confronted us at every step, but we struggled forward, dragging the wagons ourselves when the horses gave out, as they soon did, and finally, toward the end of May, we won through to a pleasant valley named Great Meadows, dominated by a mountain called Laurel Hill.
10094-8{_}1449{_}Here there was abundant forage, and as the horses could go no further, Colonel Washington ordered a halt, and determined to await the promised reinforcements.
10094-8{_}1450{_}A few days later, a company of regulars under Captain Mackay joined us, together with near a hundred men of the regiment who had remained behind with Colonel Fry, raising our numbers to four hundred men, though many were wasted with fever and dysentery.
10094-8{_}1451{_}Those of us who were able set to work throwing up a breastwork of logs, under the direction of Captain Robert Stobo, and at the end of three days had completed an inclosure a hundred feet square, with a rude cabin in the centre to hold our munitions and supplies.
10094-8{_}1452{_}There had been many alarms that the French were marching against us, but all of them had proved untrue, so when, some days after, the report spread through the camp again that the enemy were near, I paid little heed to it, and went to sleep as usual.
10094-8{_}1453{_}How long I slept, I do not know, but I was awakened by some one shaking me by the shoulder.
10094-8{_}1454{_}"Get up at once, lieutenant, and report at headquarters," said a voice I recognized as Waggoner's, and as I sat upright with a jerk, he passed on to awake another sleeper.
10094-8{_}1455{_}I was out of bed in an instant, and threw on my clothing with nervous haste.
10094-8{_}1456{_}I could hear a storm raging, and when I stepped outside the tent, I was almost blinded by the rain, driven in great sheets before the wind.
10094-8{_}1457{_}I fought my way against it to Washington's tent, where I found Captain Stephen and some thirty men, and others coming up every moment.
10094-8{_}1458{_}"What is it?"
10094-8{_}1459{_}I asked of Waggoner, who had got back to headquarters before me, but he shook his head to show that he knew no more than I.
10094-8{_}1460{_}A moment later, the flap of the tent was raised, and Colonel Washington appeared, wrapped in his cloak as though for a journey, and followed by an Indian, who, I learned afterwards, was none other than the Half King.
10094-8{_}1461{_}He spoke a few words to Captain Stephen, and the order was given to form in double rank and march, Colonel Washington himself leading the expedition, which numbered all told some forty men.
10094-8{_}1462{_}I shall never forget that midnight march through the forest, with the rain falling in a deluge through the dripping trees, the lightning flashing and the thunder rolling.
10094-8{_}1463{_}We stumbled along upon each other's heels, falling over logs or underbrush, the wet branches switching our faces raw and soaking us through and through.
10094-8{_}1464{_}It seemed to me that we must have covered fifteen or twenty miles, at least, when the first gray of the morning brightened the horizon and a halt was called, but really we had come little more than five.
10094-8{_}1465{_}Here it was found that seven men had been lost upon the way, and that our powder was so wet that most of it was useless, to many of us the charge in our firelocks being all that remained serviceable.
10094-8{_}1466{_}After an hour's halt, the order came again to march, with caution to move warily.
10094-8{_}1467{_}Scouts were thrown out ahead, and soon came back with tidings that the enemy was hard by.
10094-8{_}1468{_}My hands were trembling with excitement as we crept forward to the edge of a rocky hollow, and as we looked down the slope, we could see the French below.
10094-8{_}1469{_}There were thirty of them or more, and they were getting breakfast, their arms stacked beside them.
10094-8{_}1470{_}Almost at the same instant their sentries saw us and gave the alarm.
10094-8{_}1471{_}"Follow me, men!"
10094-8{_}1472{_}cried Washington, and he started down the slope, we after him.
10094-8{_}1473{_}As we went, the French sprang to arms and gave us a volley, but it was badly aimed in their excitement and so did little damage.
10094-8{_}1474{_}As we closed in on them we returned their fire, and some eight or nine fell, while the others, thinking doubtless that they had been surprised by a large force, threw down their guns and held up their hands in token of surrender.
10094-8{_}1475{_}Captain Stephen had been slightly wounded, but charged on down the slope ahead of us, and took prisoner a young officer, who refused to surrender, but kept on fighting until his sword was knocked from his hand.
10094-8{_}1476{_}Then he began to tear his hair and curse in French, pointing now and again to another officer who lay among the dead.
10094-8{_}1477{_}He grew so violent that he attracted Colonel Washington's attention.
10094-8{_}1478{_}"Come here a moment, Lieutenant Peyronie," he called.
10094-8{_}1479{_}"You understand French.
10094-8{_}1480{_}What is this fellow saying?"
10094-8{_}1481{_}Peyronie exchanged a few words with the prisoner, who stooped, drew a paper from the inner pocket of the dead officer's coat, and held it toward us.
10094-8{_}1482{_}Peyronie took it, glanced over it with grave countenance, and turned to Colonel Washington.
10094-8{_}1483{_}"This man is Ensign Marie Drouillon, sir," he said.
10094-8{_}1484{_}"The party was in command of Ensign Coulon de Jumonville, whom you see lying dead there.
10094-8{_}1485{_}M. Drouillon claims that the party did not come against us as spies, or for the purpose of fighting, but simply to bring a message to you from M. de Contrecoeur, who is in command of the fort at the forks of the Ohio, which, it seems, has been named Fort Duquesne.
10094-8{_}1486{_}This is the message," and he held out the paper to Washington.
10094-8{_}1487{_}"'Tis in French," said the latter, glancing over it.
10094-8{_}1488{_}"What does it say?"
10094-8{_}1489{_}"It warns you to return to the settlements," answered Peyronie, "on the pretext that all the land this side the mountains belongs to France."
10094-8{_}1490{_}Here the prisoner, who was evidently laboring under great excitement, broke in, and said something rapidly in a loud voice, which made Peyronie flush, and drew nods and cries of approbation from the other prisoners.
10094-8{_}1491{_}"What does he say?"
10094-8{_}1492{_}asked Washington, seeing that Peyronie hesitated.
10094-8{_}1493{_}"He says, sir," answered Peyronie, with evident reluctance, "that M. de Jumonville came in the character of an ambassador and has been assassinated."
10094-8{_}1494{_}Washington flushed hotly and his eyes grew dark.
10094-8{_}1495{_}"Ask M. Drouillon," he said, "why an ambassador thought it necessary to bring with him a guard of thirty men?"
10094-8{_}1496{_}Peyronie put the question, but Drouillon did not reply.
10094-8{_}1497{_}"Ask him also," continued Washington, "why he remained concealed near my troops for three days, instead of coming directly to me as an ambassador should have done?"
10094-8{_}1498{_}Again Peyronie put the question, and again there was no answer.
10094-8{_}1499{_}"Tell him," said Washington sternly, "that I see through his trick,--that I comprehend it thoroughly.
10094-8{_}1500{_}M. Jumonville counted on using his pretext of ambassador to spy upon my camp, and to avert an attack in case he was discovered.
10094-8{_}1501{_}Well, he produced his message too late.
10094-8{_}1502{_}He has behaved as an enemy, and has been treated as such.
10094-8{_}1503{_}That he is dead is wholly his own fault.
10094-8{_}1504{_}Had he chosen the part of an ambassador instead of that of a spy, this would not have happened."
10094-8{_}1505{_}He turned away, and apparently dismissed the matter from his mind, but that it troubled him long afterward I am quite certain, though in the whole affair no particle of blame attached to him.
10094-8{_}1506{_}The French made a great outcry about it, but I have never heard that any of them ever answered the questions which were put to M. Drouillon.
10094-8{_}1507{_}The truth of the matter is, that they were only too eager for some pretext upon which to base the assertion that it was the English who began hostilities, and this flimsy excuse was the best they could invent.
10094-8{_}1508{_}But that little brush under the trees on that windy May morning was to have momentous consequences, for it was the beginning of the struggle which drenched the continent in blood.
10094-8{_}1509{_}CHAPTER X  THE FRENCH SCORE FIRST   We marched back to the camp at Great Meadows with our prisoners,--some twenty in all,--much elated at our success, but near dead with fatigue.
10094-8{_}1510{_}Lieutenant Spiltdorph was selected to escort them to Virginia, and set off with them toward noon, together with twenty men, cursing the ill-luck which deprived him of the opportunity to make the remainder of the campaign with us.
10094-8{_}1511{_}For that the French would march against us in force was well-nigh certain, once they learned of Jumonville's defeat, of which the Indians would soon inform them, and that we should be outnumbered three or four to one seemed inevitable.
10094-8{_}1512{_}But no one thought of retreat, our commander, I am sure, least of all.
10094-8{_}1513{_}He seemed everywhere at once, heartening the men, inspecting equipment, overseeing the preparations for defense.
10094-8{_}1514{_}The only hostile element in the camp was the company of regulars under Captain Mackay, who refused to assist in any of the work, asserting that they were employed only to fight.
10094-8{_}1515{_}Captain Mackay, too, holding his commission from the king, claimed to outrank Colonel Washington, and yielded him but a reluctant and sullen obedience.
10094-8{_}1516{_}Christopher Gist, who had just come from Will's Creek with tidings of Colonel Fry's death, was of the opinion that a much more effective resistance might be made at his plantation, twelve miles further on, where there were some strong log buildings and a ground, so he claimed, admirably suited for intrenchment.
10094-8{_}1517{_}Accordingly, we set out for there, arriving after a fatiguing journey.
10094-8{_}1518{_}The horses were in worse case than ever, and only two miserable teams and a few tottering pack-horses remained capable of working.
10094-8{_}1519{_}Finally, on the twenty-ninth of June, the Half King, who had been our faithful friend throughout, brought us word that seven hundred French and three or four hundred Indians had marched from Fort Duquesne against us.
10094-8{_}1520{_}As the news spread through the camp, the officers left the intrenchments upon which they had been at work, and gathered to discuss the news.
10094-8{_}1521{_}There a message from Colonel Washington summoned us to a conference at Gist's cabin.
10094-8{_}1522{_}"Gentlemen," he said, when we had all assembled, "I need not tell you that the situation is most critical.
10094-8{_}1523{_}We can scarce hope to successfully oppose an enemy who outnumbers us three to one, and yet 't is impossible to retreat without abandoning all our baggage and munitions, since we have no means of transport."
10094-8{_}1524{_}He fell silent for a moment, and no one spoke.
10094-8{_}1525{_}I saw that the worry of the last few weeks had left its mark upon him, for there was a line between his eyes which I had never seen before, but which never left him afterward.
10094-8{_}1526{_}"What I propose," he said at last, "is to fall back to Great Meadows.
10094-8{_}1527{_}I believe it to be better fitted for defense than this place, which is commanded by half a dozen hills, and where we could not hope to hold out against artillery fire.
10094-8{_}1528{_}At Great Meadows we can strengthen our intrenchment in the middle of the plain, and the French will hardly dare attempt to carry it by assault, since they must advance without cover for two hundred yards or more.
10094-8{_}1529{_}It is a charming field for an encounter.
10094-8{_}1530{_}Has any one a better plan?"
10094-8{_}1531{_}Mackay was the first to speak.
10094-8{_}1532{_}"'Tis better to lose our baggage than to lose both it and our lives," he said.
10094-8{_}1533{_}"The French may not care to risk an assault, but they have only to sit down about the work for a day or two to starve us out."
10094-8{_}1534{_}"That is true," answered Washington, and his face was very grave; "yet reinforcements cannot be far distant.
10094-8{_}1535{_}Two independent companies from New York reached Annapolis a fortnight since, and are doubtless being hurried forward.
10094-8{_}1536{_}Other companies have arrived in the colony, and must be near at hand.
10094-8{_}1537{_}Besides," he added, in a firmer tone, "I cannot consent to return to Virginia without striking at least one blow at the French, else this expedition might just as well have never been begun."
10094-8{_}1538{_}"That is the point!"
10094-8{_}1539{_}cried Stephen.
10094-8{_}1540{_}"Let us not run away until we see something to run from.
10094-8{_}1541{_}Your plan is the best possible under the circumstances, Colonel Washington."
10094-8{_}1542{_}We all of us echoed this opinion, and after thanking us warmly, our commander bade us make ready at once for the return to Great Meadows.
10094-8{_}1543{_}The baggage was done into packs as large as a man could carry; a force was told off to drag the swivels; the officers added their horses to the train, and prepared to carry packs just as the men did.
10094-8{_}1544{_}Colonel Washington left half of his personal baggage behind, paying some soldiers four pistoles to carry the remainder.
10094-8{_}1545{_}So at daybreak we set out, the sufferings of our men being greatly aggravated by the conduct of the regulars, who refused to carry a pound of baggage or place a hand upon the ropes by which we dragged our guns after us.
10094-8{_}1546{_}The miseries of that day I hope never to see repeated.
10094-8{_}1547{_}Men dropped senseless on the road, or fell beneath the trees, unable to go further.
10094-8{_}1548{_}The main body of the troops struggled on, leaving these stragglers to follow when they could, and on the morning of the next day we reached Great Meadows, weak, trembling, and exhausted.
10094-8{_}1549{_}But even here there was no rest for us, for it was necessary to strengthen our defenses against the attack which could not be long deferred.
10094-8{_}1550{_}The breastwork seemed all too weak now we knew the force which would be brought against it, and we started to dig a trench around it, but so feeble were the men that it was only half completed.
10094-8{_}1551{_}Even at the best, our condition was little short of desperate.
10094-8{_}1552{_}Much of our ammunition had been ruined, and our supply of provisions was near gone.
10094-8{_}1553{_}We had been without bread for above a week, and while we had plenty of cattle for beef, we had no salt with which to cure the meat, and the hot summer sun soon made it unfit to eat.
10094-8{_}1554{_}Yet, with all this, there was little murmuring, the example of our commander encouraging us all.
10094-8{_}1555{_}At our council in our tent that evening, Peyronie, with invincible good humor, declared that no man could complain so long as the tobacco lasted, and in a cloud of blue-gray smoke, we gave our hastily constructed fort the suggestive name of "Fort Necessity."
10094-8{_}1556{_}The morning of the third of July was spent by us in overhauling the firelocks and making the last dispositions of our men.
10094-8{_}1557{_}Colonel Washington inspected personally the whole line, and saw that no detail was overlooked.
10094-8{_}1558{_}He had not slept for two nights, but seemed indefatigable, and even the regulars cheered him as he passed along the breastwork.
10094-8{_}1559{_}But at last the inspection was finished and we settled down to wait.
10094-8{_}1560{_}Peyronie and myself had been stationed at the northwest corner of the fort with thirty men, and just before noon, from far away in the forest, came the sound of a single musket shot.
10094-8{_}1561{_}We waited in suspense for what might follow, and in a moment a sentry came running from the wood with one arm swinging useless by his side.
10094-8{_}1562{_}"They have come!"
10094-8{_}1563{_}he cried, as he tumbled over the breastwork.
10094-8{_}1564{_}"They will be here in a moment," and even as he spoke, the edge of the forest was filled with French and Indians, and a lively fire was opened against us, but the range was so great that the bullets did no damage.
10094-8{_}1565{_}The drums beat the alarm, and expecting a general attack, we were formed in column before the intrenchment.
10094-8{_}1566{_}But the enemy had no stomach for that kind of work, and veered off to the south, where they occupied two little hills, whence they could enfilade a portion of our position.
10094-8{_}1567{_}We answered their fire as best we could, but it was cruel, disheartening work.
10094-8{_}1568{_}"Do you call this war?"
10094-8{_}1569{_}asked Peyronie impatiently, after an hour of this gunnery.
10094-8{_}1570{_}"In faith, had I thought 'twould be like this, I had been less eager to enlist.
10094-8{_}1571{_}Why don't the cowards try an assault?"
10094-8{_}1572{_}"Yes, why don't they?"
10094-8{_}1573{_}and I looked gloomily at the wall of trees from which jets of smoke and flame puffed incessantly.
10094-8{_}1574{_}"'Tis not the kind of fighting I've been used to," cried Peyronie.
10094-8{_}1575{_}"In Europe we fight on open ground, where the best man wins; we do not skulk behind the trees and through the underbrush.
10094-8{_}1576{_}I've a good notion to try a sally.
10094-8{_}1577{_}What say you, Stewart?"
10094-8{_}1578{_}"Here comes Colonel Washington," I answered.
10094-8{_}1579{_}"Let us ask him."
10094-8{_}1580{_}But he shook his head when we proposed it to him.
10094-8{_}1581{_}"'Twould be madness," he said.
10094-8{_}1582{_}"They are three times our number, and would pick us all off before we could reach the trees.
10094-8{_}1583{_}No, the best we can do is to remain behind our breastwork.
10094-8{_}1584{_}It seems a mean kind of warfare, I admit, but 'tis a kind we must get accustomed to, if we are to fight the French and Indians;" and he walked on along his rounds, speaking a word of encouragement here and there, and seemingly quite unconscious of the bullets which whistled about him.
10094-8{_}1585{_}Yet the breastwork did not protect us wholly, for now and then a man would throw up his arms and fall with a single shrill cry, or roll over in the mud of the trench, cursing horribly, with a bullet in him somewhere.
10094-8{_}1586{_}Doctor Craik, who had enlisted as lieutenant, was soon compelled to lay aside his gun and do what he could to relieve their suffering.
10094-8{_}1587{_}Not for a moment during the afternoon did the enemy's fire slacken, and the strain began to tell upon our men.
10094-8{_}1588{_}The pieces grew foul, there were only two screw-rods in the camp with which to clean them, and as the hours passed, our fire grew less and less.
10094-8{_}1589{_}The swivels had long since been abandoned, for the gunners were picked off so soon as they showed themselves above the breastwork.
10094-8{_}1590{_}There had been mutterings of thunder and dashes of rain all the afternoon, and now the storm broke in earnest, the rain falling in such fury as I had never seen.
10094-8{_}1591{_}The trenches filled with water, and we tried in vain to keep dry the powder in our cartouch boxes.
10094-8{_}1592{_}Not only was this wet, but the rain leaked through the magazine we had built in the middle of the camp, and ruined the ammunition we had stored there.
10094-8{_}1593{_}So soon as the rain slackened, the enemy resumed their fire, but Major Washington forbade us to reply, since there was scarce a dozen rounds in the fort.
10094-8{_}1594{_}I confess that this species of fighting took the heart out of me, and I could see no chance of a successful issue.
10094-8{_}1595{_}I was sitting thus, looking gloomily out at the forest in front of me, and wondering why the fire from there had ceased, when I noticed that there seemed to be many more rocks and bushes scattered about the plain than I had ever before observed.
10094-8{_}1596{_}The gloom of the evening had fallen, and I rubbed my eyes and looked again to make sure I was not mistaken.
10094-8{_}1597{_}No, there was no mistake, and I suddenly understood what was about to happen.
10094-8{_}1598{_}"Peyronie," I whispered to my neighbor, who was sitting in the mud, swearing softly under his mustache, "we are going to have some excitement presently.
10094-8{_}1599{_}The Indians are creeping up to carry us by assault."
10094-8{_}1600{_}"What?"
10094-8{_}1601{_}he exclaimed, sitting suddenly upright.
10094-8{_}1602{_}"Oh, no such luck!"
10094-8{_}1603{_}"Yes, but they are," I insisted.
10094-8{_}1604{_}"Watch those bushes out there.
10094-8{_}1605{_}See, they 're moving up toward us."
10094-8{_}1606{_}He rose to his knees and peered keenly out through the gloom.
10094-8{_}1607{_}"Pardieu," he muttered after a moment, "so they are!
10094-8{_}1608{_}Well, we shall be ready for them."
10094-8{_}1609{_}We passed the word around to our men, and startled them into new life.
10094-8{_}1610{_}The muskets were primed sparingly with dry powder, and we waited with tense nerves for the assault.
10094-8{_}1611{_}The fusillade from the hills had been redoubled, but a terrible and threatening silence hung over the intrenchment, and doubtless encouraged our assailants to believe that our ammunition was quite gone.
10094-8{_}1612{_}Near and nearer crept the Indians, fifty or sixty of them at least, and perhaps many more, and we lay still with bursting pulses and waited.
10094-8{_}1613{_}Now the foremost of them was scarce forty yards away, and suddenly, with a yell, they were all upon their feet and charging us.
10094-8{_}1614{_}"Tirez, tirez!"
10094-8{_}1615{_}shouted Peyronie, forgetting his English in his excitement, and we sent a volley full into them.
10094-8{_}1616{_}It was a warmer reception than they had counted on, and they wavered for a moment, but there must have been a Frenchman leading them, for they rallied, and came on again with a rush.
10094-8{_}1617{_}We met them with fixed bayonets, but they outnumbered us so greatly that we must have given way before them had not Colonel Washington, hearing the uproar and guessing its meaning, dashed over at the head of reinforcements and given them another volley.
10094-8{_}1618{_}As I was reloading with feverish haste, I saw an Indian rush at Colonel Washington with raised tomahawk.
10094-8{_}1619{_}Washington raised his pistol, coolly took aim, and pulled the trigger, but the powder flashed and did not explode.
10094-8{_}1620{_}With the sweat starting from my forehead, I dashed some powder into the pan of my pistol, jerked it up, and fired.
10094-8{_}1621{_}Ah, Captain Paul, how I blessed your lessons in that moment!
10094-8{_}1622{_}for the ball went true, and the Indian rolled in the mud almost at Washington's feet.
10094-8{_}1623{_}They had had enough, and those who were still alive leaped the trench and disappeared into the outer darkness.
10094-8{_}1624{_}"They won't try that again," I remarked to Peyronie, who was sitting against the breastwork.
10094-8{_}1625{_}"But what is it, man?
10094-8{_}1626{_}Are you wounded?"
10094-8{_}1627{_}I cried, seeing that he was very pale and held both hands to his breast.
10094-8{_}1628{_}"Yes, I am hit here," he answered, and added, as I fell on my knees beside him and began to tear the clothing from the wound, "but do not distress yourself, Stewart.
10094-8{_}1629{_}I can be attended after the battle is won."
10094-8{_}1630{_}"Nonsense," I said.
10094-8{_}1631{_}"You shall be attended at once."
10094-8{_}1632{_}He smiled up at me, and then went suddenly white and fell against my shoulder.
10094-8{_}1633{_}I tore away his shirt, and saw that blood was welling from a wound in the breast.
10094-8{_}1634{_}I propped him against the wall, and ordering one of the men to go for Doctor Craik, stanched the blood as well as I could.
10094-8{_}1635{_}The doctor hastened to us so soon as he could leave his other wounded, but he shook his head gravely when he saw Peyronie's injury.
10094-8{_}1636{_}"A bad case," he said.
10094-8{_}1637{_}"Clear into the lungs, I think.
10094-8{_}1638{_}But I have seen men recover of worse hurts," he added, seeing how pale I was.
10094-8{_}1639{_}I watched him as he bound up the wound with deft fingers, and then between us we carried him to the little cabin, which had been converted from magazine to hospital, and was already crowded from wall to wall.
10094-8{_}1640{_}It was with a sore heart that I left him and returned to the breastwork, for I had come to love Peyronie dearly.
10094-8{_}1641{_}The event was not so serious as I then feared, for, after a gallant fight for life, he won the battle, recovered of his wound, and lived to do service in another war.
10094-8{_}1642{_}The repulse of the Indians seemed to have disheartened the enemy, for their fire slackened until only a shot now and then broke the stillness of the night.
10094-8{_}1643{_}Our condition was desperate as it could well be, yet I heard no word of surrender.
10094-8{_}1644{_}I was sitting listlessly, thinking of Peyronie's wound, when a whisper ran along the lines that the French were sending a flag of truce.
10094-8{_}1645{_}Sure enough, we could see a man in white uniform approaching the breastwork, waving a white flag above his head.
10094-8{_}1646{_}He was halted by the sentries while yet some distance off, and Colonel Washington sent for.
10094-8{_}1647{_}He appeared in a moment.
10094-8{_}1648{_}"Where is Lieutenant Peyronie?"
10094-8{_}1649{_}he asked.
10094-8{_}1650{_}"We will have need of him."
10094-8{_}1651{_}"He is wounded, sir," I answered.
10094-8{_}1652{_}"He was shot through the breast during the assault."
10094-8{_}1653{_}Washington glanced about at the circle of faces.
10094-8{_}1654{_}"Is there any other here who speaks French?"
10094-8{_}1655{_}he asked.
10094-8{_}1656{_}There was a moment's silence.
10094-8{_}1657{_}"Why, sir," said Vanbraam at last, "I have managed to pick up the fag ends of a good many languages during my life, and I can jabber French a little."
10094-8{_}1658{_}"Very well," and Washington motioned him forward.
10094-8{_}1659{_}"Mount the breastwork and ask this fellow what he wants."
10094-8{_}1660{_}Vanbraam did as he was bid, and there was a moment's high-toned conversation between him and the Frenchman.
10094-8{_}1661{_}"He says, sir," said Vanbraam, "that he has been sent by his commander, M. Coulon-Villiers, to propose a parley."
10094-8{_}1662{_}Washington looked at him keenly.
10094-8{_}1663{_}"And he wishes to enter the fort?"
10094-8{_}1664{_}"He says he wishes to see you, sir."
10094-8{_}1665{_}Washington glanced about at the mud-filled trenches, the ragged, weary men, the haggard faces of the officers, the dead scattered here and there along the breastwork, and his face grew stern.
10094-8{_}1666{_}"'Tis a trick!"
10094-8{_}1667{_}he cried.
10094-8{_}1668{_}"He wishes to see how we are situated.
10094-8{_}1669{_}Tell him that we do not care to parley, but are well prepared to defend ourselves against any force the French can muster."
10094-8{_}1670{_}I gasped at the audacity of the man, and the Frenchman was doubtless no less astonished.
10094-8{_}1671{_}He disappeared into the forest, but half an hour later again approached the fort.
10094-8{_}1672{_}Vanbraam's services as interpreter were called for a second time, and there was a longer parley between him and the messenger.
10094-8{_}1673{_}"He proposes," said Vanbraam, when the talk was finished, "that we send two officers to meet two French officers, for the purpose of agreeing upon articles of capitulation.
10094-8{_}1674{_}M. Coulon-Villiers states that he is prepared to make many concessions, and he believes this course will be for the advantage of both parties."
10094-8{_}1675{_}Washington looked around at the officers grouped about him.
10094-8{_}1676{_}"It is clear that we must endeavor to make terms, gentlemen," he said.
10094-8{_}1677{_}"The morning will disclose our plight to the enemy, and it will then be no longer a question of terms, but of surrender.
10094-8{_}1678{_}At present they believe us capable of defense, hence they talk of concessions.
10094-8{_}1679{_}What say you, gentlemen?"
10094-8{_}1680{_}There was nothing to be said except to agree, and Vanbraam and Captain Stephen were sent out to confer with the French.
10094-8{_}1681{_}They returned in the course of an hour, bringing with them the articles already signed by Coulon-Villiers, and awaiting only Colonel Washington's ratification.
10094-8{_}1682{_}Vanbraam read them aloud by the light of a flickering candle, and we listened in silence until he had finished.
10094-8{_}1683{_}They were better than we could have hoped, providing that we should march out at daybreak with all the honors of war, drums beating, flags flying, and match lighted for our cannon; that we should take with us our baggage, be protected from the Indians, and be permitted to retire unmolested to Virginia, in return for which we were to release all the prisoners we had taken a few days before, and as they were already on their way to the colony, should leave two officers with the French as hostages until the prisoners had been delivered to them.
10094-8{_}1684{_}There was a moment's silence when Vanbraam had finished reading, and then, without raising his head, Colonel Washington signed, and threw the pen far from him.
10094-8{_}1685{_}Then he arose and walked slowly to his quarters, and I saw him no more that night.
10094-8{_}1686{_}Captain Mackay insisted also that he must sign the paper, and, to my intense disgust, wrote his name in above that of our commander.
10094-8{_}1687{_}There was little sleep for any of us that night, and I almost envied Peyronie tossing on his blanket, oblivious to what was passing about him.
10094-8{_}1688{_}Vanbraam and Robert Stobo were appointed to accompany the French back to the Ohio, to remain there as hostages, and we all shook hands with them before they went away through the darkness toward the French camp.
10094-8{_}1689{_}But the night passed, and at daybreak we abandoned the fort and began the retreat, carrying our sick and wounded on our backs, since the Indians had killed all our horses.
10094-8{_}1690{_}Most of our baggage was perforce left behind, and the Indians lost no time in looting it.
10094-8{_}1691{_}That done, they pressed threateningly upon our rear, so that an attack seemed imminent, nor did the French make any effort to restrain them; but we held firm, and the Indians finally drew off and returned to the fort, leaving us to cover as best we might those weary miles over the mountains.
10094-8{_}1692{_}By the promise of ten pistoles, I had secured two men to bear Peyronie between them on a blanket, but 'twas impossible to treat all the wounded so, and the fainting men staggered along under their screaming burdens, falling sometimes, and lying where they fell from sheer exhaustion.
10094-8{_}1693{_}What Colonel Washington's feelings were I could only guess.
10094-8{_}1694{_}He strode at the head of the column, his head bowed on his breast, his heart doubtless torn by the suffering about him, and saying not a word for hours together, nor did any venture to approach him.
10094-8{_}1695{_}I doubt if ever in his life he will be called upon to pass through a darker hour than he did on that morning of the fourth of July, 1754.
10094-8{_}1696{_}Through no fault of his, the power of England on the Ohio had been dealt a staggering blow, and his pride and ambition crushed into the dust.
10094-8{_}1697{_}What need to tell of that weary march back to the settlements, the suffering by the way, the sorry reception accorded us, the consternation caused by the news of French success?
10094-8{_}1698{_}At Winchester we met two companies from North Carolina which had been marching to join us, and these were ordered to Will's Creek, to establish a post to protect the frontier from the expected Indian aggression.
10094-8{_}1699{_}Captain Mackay and his men remained at Winchester, while our regiment returned to Alexandria to rest and recruit.
10094-8{_}1700{_}As for me, I was glad enough to put off the harness of war and make the best of my way back to Riverview, saddened and humbled by this first experience, which was so different from the warfare of which I had read and dreamed, with its bright pageantry, its charges and shock of arms, its feats of single combat.
10094-8{_}1701{_}Fate willed that I was yet to see another, trained on the battlefields of Europe, humbled in the dust by these foes whom I found so despicable, and the soldiers of the king taught a lesson they were never to forget.
10094-8{_}1702{_}One word more.
10094-8{_}1703{_}Perhaps I have been unjust to Captain Mackay and his men.
10094-8{_}1704{_}Time has done much to soften the bitterness with which their conduct filled me, and as I look back now across the score of years that lie between, I can appreciate to some degree their attitude toward our commander.
10094-8{_}1705{_}Certainly it might seem a dangerous thing to intrust an enterprise of such moment to a youth of twenty-two, with no knowledge of warfare but that he had gained from books.
10094-8{_}1706{_}It is perhaps not wonderful that veterans should have looked at him askance, and I would not think of them too harshly.
10094-8{_}1707{_}He doubtless made mistakes,--as what man would not have done?--yet I believe that not even the first captain of the empire could have snatched victory from odds so desperate.
10094-8{_}1708{_}CHAPTER XI  DREAM DAYS AT RIVERVIEW   In the many summer evenings which followed, I played the part of that broken soldier, who, as Mr. Goldsmith tells us so delightfully,  "talked the night away, Wept o'er his wounds, or, tales of sorrow done, Shouldered his crutch and showed how fields were won."
10094-8{_}1709{_}Alas, I could show not how they were won, but only how they were lost, and how was one to clothe in romance a battle which had been fought in the midst of mud and rain, from behind a breastwork, and with scarce a glimpse of the enemy?
10094-8{_}1710{_}But I had a rapt audience of two in James and Dorothy.
10094-8{_}1711{_}They were not critical, and I told the story of Great Meadows over and over again, a score of times.
10094-8{_}1712{_}A hundred yards from the house, overlooking on one side the willow-draped waters of Occoquan Inlet, and on the other the broad and placid river, a seat had been fashioned between two massive oaks, and here, of an evening, it was our wont to go.
10094-8{_}1713{_}Sometimes, by great good fortune, James did not accompany us, and Dorothy and I would sit there alone together and watch the shadows deepen across the water.
10094-8{_}1714{_}Our talk would falter and die away before the beauty of the scene, and there would be long silences, broken only now and then by a half whispered sentence.
10094-8{_}1715{_}I had never known a sweeter time, and even yet, when night is coming on, I love to steal forth to sit there again and gaze across the water and dream upon the past.
10094-8{_}1716{_}During the day, I saw but little of the other members of the family, and was left greatly to my own resources.
10094-8{_}1717{_}My aunt was ever busy with the management of the estate, to every detail of which she gave personal attention, and which she administered with a thrift and thoroughness I could not but admire.
10094-8{_}1718{_}The worry of incessant business left its mark upon her.
10094-8{_}1719{_}The lines in her face deepened, and the silver in her hair grew more pronounced, but though she doubtless felt her strength failing, she clung grimly to the work.
10094-8{_}1720{_}I would have offered to assist her but that I knew she would resent the suggestion, and would believe I made it to gain some knowledge of the income from the estate, of which I had always been kept in densest ignorance, and with which, indeed, I troubled myself but little.
10094-8{_}1721{_}I think her old fear of my claiming the place came on her again, and though she always tried to treat me civilly, the effort in the end proved too great for her overwrought nerves, as you shall presently hear.
10094-8{_}1722{_}Upon Dorothy fell the duty of looking after the household, and she went about it cheerfully and willingly.
10094-8{_}1723{_}Her mornings were passed in instructing the servants in their duties and seeing that their work was properly done.
10094-8{_}1724{_}There were visits to the pantry and kitchen, and a long conference with the cook, so that noon was soon at hand.
10094-8{_}1725{_}The afternoon was spent in the great workroom on the upper floor, into which I ventured to peep once or twice, only to be bidden to go about my business.
10094-8{_}1726{_}But it was a pleasant sight, and I sometimes gathered courage to steal down the corridor for a glimpse of it.
10094-8{_}1727{_}There sat Dorothy in a dainty gown of Covent Garden calico, directing half a dozen old negro women, who were cutting out and sewing together the winter clothing of fearnaught for the slaves.
10094-8{_}1728{_}Two or three girls had been brought in to be taught the mysteries of needle-craft, and Dorothy turned to them from time to time to watch their work and direct their rebellious fingers.
10094-8{_}1729{_}I would fain have taken a lesson, too, but when I proposed this one day, representing how great my need might be when I was over the mountains far away from any woman, Dorothy informed me sternly, amid the titters of the others, that my fingers were too big and clumsy to be taught to manage so delicate an instrument as a needle, and sent me from the room.
10094-8{_}1730{_}Young James had also much to occupy his time.
10094-8{_}1731{_}His mother was as yet in doubt whether he should complete his education at William and Mary, as I had done, or should be sent to London to acquire the true polish.
10094-8{_}1732{_}The boy greatly favored the latter course, as any boy of spirit would have done, and his mother would have yielded to him readily, but for the stories she had heard of the riotous living which prevailed among the young blades in London, and of which she had had ample confirmation from Parson Scott, who, I suspect, before coming to his estate at Westwood, had ruffled it with the best of them.
10094-8{_}1733{_}Whether it should be Williamsburg or London, the boy was required to be kept at his books every morning, and was off every afternoon to the Dumfries tavern, where there was always a crowd of ne'er-do-wells, promoting a cock-fight, or a horse race, or eye-gouging contest.
10094-8{_}1734{_}Sometimes, he elected to spend the evening in this company, and it was then that Dorothy and I were left alone together on the seat beside the river.
10094-8{_}1735{_}But when Sunday came, there was another story.
10094-8{_}1736{_}The great coach was brought from the stable and polished till it shone again,--indeed, it had been polished so often and so vigorously that its gilding and paint began to show the marks of it.
10094-8{_}1737{_}The four horses were led out, rubbed down from nose to heel, and harnessed in their brightest trappings.
10094-8{_}1738{_}The driver, footman, and two outriders donned their liveries, in which they were the envy of all the other servants, and the coach was driven around to the front of the house, from which presently emerged Madame Stewart, in a stately gown of flowered calamanco, her fan and gold pomander in her hand.
10094-8{_}1739{_}Then came Dorothy, her sweet face looking most coquettish under her Ranelagh mob of gauze, the ribbons crossed beneath her chin and fluttering half a yard behind.
10094-8{_}1740{_}As she tripped down the stops and lifted her tiffany petticoat ever so little, I could catch a glimpse of the prettiest pair of ankles in the world in silk-clocked hose, for the reader can guess without my telling that I was close behind, holding her kerchief or her fan or her silver tui until she should be safely seated in the coach.
10094-8{_}1741{_}And that once done, the whip cracked, the wheels started, and I swung myself on horseback and trotted along beside the window, on Dorothy's side, you may be sure.
10094-8{_}1742{_}So, in great state, we proceeded to the new Quantico church near Dumfries, a prodigious fine structure of brick, built the year before at a cost of a hundred thousand weight of tobacco, of which my aunt had contributed a tenth.
10094-8{_}1743{_}The other members of the congregation awaited our arrival, grouped before the door, and, entering after us, remained decently standing till we had mounted to the loft and taken our seats, a show of deference which greatly pleased my aunt.
10094-8{_}1744{_}The church was built in a little recess from the road, in the midst of a grove of ancient trees, cruciform, as so many others were throughout the colony, and stands today just as it stood then,--as I have good cause to know, for 't was in that church, before that altar--But there, you shall learn it all in time.
10094-8{_}1745{_}Doctor Scott was a goodly preacher, but the one portion of the service for me was the singing, when I might stand beside Dorothy and listen to her voice.
10094-8{_}1746{_}She sang with whole heart and undivided mind, recking nothing of me standing spellbound there.
10094-8{_}1747{_}Indeed, I think the pastor shrewdly saw that her singing was a means of grace no less than his expounding, and he never failed to journey to Riverview on a Friday to talk over with her what should be her part in the service on the coming Sunday.
10094-8{_}1748{_}Nor did I ever know her to refuse this labor,--not because she was vain of her power, but because she saw the good it did.
10094-8{_}1749{_}The service once over, there were greetings to exchange, the news of the neighborhood to talk over, crops to discuss, and what not.
10094-8{_}1750{_}My heart would burn within me as I saw the men buzzing about Dorothy like flies about a dish of honey, though my jealousy was lightened when I saw that while she had a gay word for each of them, she smiled on all alike.
10094-8{_}1751{_}The minx could read my mind like an open book, whether I was moping in one corner of the churchyard or on the bench beside her, and she loved to tease me by pretending great admiration for this man or that, and consulting me about him as she would have done a brother.
10094-8{_}1752{_}Which, I need hardly say, annoyed me vastly.
10094-8{_}1753{_}The gossip over, we drove home again to lunch, after which, on the wide veranda or the bench by the river's edge, I would read Dorothy some bits of Mr. Addison or Mr. Pope, which latter she could not abide, though his pungent verses fell in exceeding well with my melancholy humor.
10094-8{_}1754{_}Evening past and bedtime come, I lighted Dorothy's candle for her at the table in the lower hall, where the silver sticks were set out in their nightly array like French soldiers, gleaming all in white, and when I gave it to her and bade her good-night at the stair-foot, I got her hand to hold for an instant.
10094-8{_}1755{_}Then to my room, where over innumerable pipes of sweet-scented, I struggled with some halting verses of my own until my candle guttered in its stick.
10094-8{_}1756{_}Hours and hours did I pass thinking how I might tell her of my love, but at the last I concluded it were better to say nothing, until I had something more to offer her.
10094-8{_}1757{_}What right had I, I questioned bitterly, to offer marriage to any maid, when I had no home to which to take a wife, and I had never felt the irksomeness of my circumstances as I did at that moment.
10094-8{_}1758{_}Something of my thought she must have understood, for she was very kind to me, and never by any word or act showed that she thought of the poverty of my condition.
10094-8{_}1759{_}So August and September passed, and great events were stirring.
10094-8{_}1760{_}The House of Burgesses had met, and had been much impressed by the showing we had made against the French, so that they passed a vote of thanks to Colonel Washington for his distinguished services, and to the officers and men who had been with him.
10094-8{_}1761{_}Dinwiddie was most eager that another advance should be made at once against Duquesne, but Colonel Washington pointed out how hopeless any such attempt must be against the overwhelming odds the enemy would bring against us.
10094-8{_}1762{_}The news of French aggression on the Ohio and of our defeat at Fort Necessity had opened the eyes of the court to the danger which threatened the colonies, and great preparations were set on foot for an expedition to be sent to Virginia in the early spring.
10094-8{_}1763{_}Parliament voted 50,000 toward its expenses, and it was proposed to equip it on such a scale that the French could not hope to stand before it.
10094-8{_}1764{_}So it was decided that nothing more should be attempted by the colony until the forces from England had arrived.
10094-8{_}1765{_}And then, one day, came the astounding news that Colonel Washington had resigned from the service and returned to Mount Vernon.
10094-8{_}1766{_}A negro whom Dorothy had sent on some errand to Betty Washington had brought the news back with him.
10094-8{_}1767{_}I could scarcely credit it, and was soon galloping toward Mount Vernon to confirm it for myself.
10094-8{_}1768{_}I dare say the ten miles of river road were never more quickly covered.
10094-8{_}1769{_}As I turned into the broad graveled way which led past the garden up to the house, I saw a tall and well-known figure standing before the door, and he came toward me with a smile as I threw myself from the saddle.
10094-8{_}1770{_}"Ah, Tom," he cried, "I thought I should see you soon," and he took my hand warmly.
10094-8{_}1771{_}"Is it true," I asked, too anxious to delay an instant the solution of the mystery, "that you have left the service?"
10094-8{_}1772{_}"Yes, it is true."
10094-8{_}1773{_}"And you will not make the campaign?"
10094-8{_}1774{_}"I see no prospect now of doing so."
10094-8{_}1775{_}"But why?"
10094-8{_}1776{_}I asked.
10094-8{_}1777{_}"Pardon me, if I am indiscreet."
10094-8{_}1778{_}"'Tis a reason which all may know," and he smiled grimly, "which, indeed, I wish all to know, that my action may not be misjudged."
10094-8{_}1779{_}We were walking up and down before the door, and he paused a moment as though to choose his words, lest he say more than he desired.
10094-8{_}1780{_}"You know there has been great unpleasantness," he said at last, "between officers holding royal commissions and those holding provincial ones, concerning the matter of precedence.
10094-8{_}1781{_}You may remember that Captain Mackay held himself my superior at Fort Necessity, because he had his commission from the crown."
10094-8{_}1782{_}Of course I remembered it, as well as the many disagreements which the contention had occasioned.
10094-8{_}1783{_}"It was evident that the question must be settled one way or another," continued Washington, "and to do this, an order has just been issued by the governor.
10094-8{_}1784{_}The order provides that no officer who does not derive his commission immediately from the king can command one who does."
10094-8{_}1785{_}It was some minutes before I understood the full effect which such an order would have.
10094-8{_}1786{_}"Do you mean," I asked at last, "that you would be outranked by every subaltern in the service who holds a royal commission?"
10094-8{_}1787{_}"Unquestionably," and Washington looked away across the fields with a stern face.
10094-8{_}1788{_}"But that is an outrage!"
10094-8{_}1789{_}I cried.
10094-8{_}1790{_}"What, every whippersnapper in the line be your superior?
10094-8{_}1791{_}Why, it's rank folly!"
10094-8{_}1792{_}"So I thought," said Washington, "and therefore I resigned, and refused to serve under such conditions."
10094-8{_}1793{_}"And you did right," I said warmly.
10094-8{_}1794{_}"You could have taken no other course."
10094-8{_}1795{_}But much pressure was brought to bear upon him to get him back into the service.
10094-8{_}1796{_}General Sharpe was most anxious to secure the services of the best fighter and most experienced soldier in Virginia, and urged him to accept a company of the Virginia troops; but he replied shortly that, though strongly bent to arms, he had no inclination to hold a commission to which neither rank nor emolument attached.
10094-8{_}1797{_}And that remained his answer to all like importunities.
10094-8{_}1798{_}Whereat the authorities were greatly wroth at him, from Governor Dinwiddie down, and seeking how they might wound him further, cut from the rolls the names of half a dozen officers whom they knew to be his friends.
10094-8{_}1799{_}I was one of those who got a discharge, the reason alleged in my case being that the companies had been so reduced in number that there was not need of so many officers.
10094-8{_}1800{_}It was a heavy blow to me, I admit, and I think for a time Washington wavered in his purpose; but his friends, of whom many now came to Mount Vernon, persuaded him to remain firm in his resolution, confident that when the commander-in-chief arrived and learned how matters stood, he would make every reparation in his power.
10094-8{_}1801{_}At the bottom of the entire trouble was, I think, Dinwiddie's jealousy of Washington's growing popularity and influence, a jealousy which had been roused by every man who had come into great favor with the people since Dinwiddie had been lieutenant-governor of Virginia.
10094-8{_}1802{_}During the months that followed I was much at Mount Vernon.
10094-8{_}1803{_}Indeed, it was during that winter that we formed the warm attachment which still continues.
10094-8{_}1804{_}The family life there attracted me greatly, and I cannot sufficiently express my admiration for Mrs. Washington.
10094-8{_}1805{_}She was slight and delicate of figure, but not even her eldest son, who towered above her, possessed a greater dignity or grace.
10094-8{_}1806{_}I loved to sit at one corner of the great fireplace and see her eyes kindle with pride and affection as she gazed at him, nor did her other children love him less than she.
10094-8{_}1807{_}With the new year came renewed reports of activity in England.
10094-8{_}1808{_}Two regiments under command of Major-General Braddock were to be sent to Virginia, whence, after being enforced by provincial levies, they were to march against the French.
10094-8{_}1809{_}I need not say how both Colonel Washington and myself chafed at the thought that we were not to make the campaign; but when he suggested accepting a commission as captain of the provincial troops, his friends protested so against it that he finally abandoned the idea for good and all, and we settled down to bear the inactivity as best we could.
10094-8{_}1810{_}But at last the summons came.
10094-8{_}1811{_}It was Colonel Washington's twenty-third birthday, and there was quite a celebration at Mount Vernon.
10094-8{_}1812{_}The members of the family were all there, as were Dorothy, her brother, and myself, as well as many other friends from farther down the neck.
10094-8{_}1813{_}Dinner was served in the long, low-ceilinged dining-room, with the wide fireplace in one corner.
10094-8{_}1814{_}What a meal it was, with Mrs. Washington at the table-head and her son at the foot, yes, and Dorothy there beside me with the brightest of bright eyes!
10094-8{_}1815{_}I was ever a good trencherman, and never did venison, wild turkey, and great yellow sweet potatoes taste more savorsome than they did that day, with a jar of Mrs. Washington's marmalade for relish.
10094-8{_}1816{_}At the end came Pompey with a great steaming bowl of flip, and as the mugs were filled and passed from hand to hand, Dorothy and Betty Washington plunged in the red-hot irons with great hissing and sizzle and an aroma most delicious.
10094-8{_}1817{_}We pledged our host, the ladies sipping from our cups--need I say who from mine?--with little startled cries of agitation when the liquor stung them.
10094-8{_}1818{_}Then they left us to our pipes; but before the smoke was fairly started, there came the gallop of a horse up the roadway past the kitchen garden, and a moment later the great brass knocker was plied by a vigorous hand.
10094-8{_}1819{_}We sat in mute expectancy, and presently old Pompey thrust in his head.
10094-8{_}1820{_}"Gen'leman t' see you, sah," he said to Colonel Washington.
10094-8{_}1821{_}"Show him in here, Pomp," said the colonel; and a moment later one of the governor's messengers entered, booted and spurred, his clothing splashed with mud.
10094-8{_}1822{_}"I have a message for you from the governor, Colonel Washington," he said, saluting, and holding out a letter bearing the governor's great seal.
10094-8{_}1823{_}Washington took it without a trace of emotion, though I doubt not his heart was beating as madly as my own.
10094-8{_}1824{_}"Sit down, sir," he said heartily to the messenger, "and taste our punch.
10094-8{_}1825{_}I am sure you will find it excellent;" and when he had seen him seated and served, he turned away to the window and opened the letter.
10094-8{_}1826{_}I watched him eagerly as he read it, and saw a slow flush steal into his cheeks.
10094-8{_}1827{_}"There is nothing here I may not tell, gentlemen," he said after a moment, turning back to the group about the table.
10094-8{_}1828{_}"Governor Dinwiddie writes me that General Braddock and the first of the transports have arrived safely off Hampton, and that he desires me to meet him in Williamsburg as soon as possible, as he thinks my knowledge of the country may be of some value.
10094-8{_}1829{_}I shall start in the morning," he added, turning to the messenger.
10094-8{_}1830{_}"I trust you will remain and be our guest till then."
10094-8{_}1831{_}"Gladly," answered the man, "and ride back with you."
10094-8{_}1832{_}So it was settled.
10094-8{_}1833{_}We were not long away from the women after that, for they must hear the great news.
10094-8{_}1834{_}Colonel Washington refused to speculate about it, but I was certain he was to be proffered some employment in the coming campaign commensurate with his merit.
10094-8{_}1835{_}The afternoon passed all too quickly, and the moment came for us to start back to Riverview.
10094-8{_}1836{_}Dorothy ran upstairs to don her safeguard, the horses were brought out, and James and I struggled into our coats.
10094-8{_}1837{_}Dorothy was back in a moment, kissed Mrs. Washington and Betty, and I helped her adjust her mask and lifted her to the saddle.
10094-8{_}1838{_}I felt my cheeks burning as I turned to bid good-by to Colonel Washington, who had followed us from the house.
10094-8{_}1839{_}"If it should be an appointment," I began, as I grasped his hand.
10094-8{_}1840{_}"You maybe sure I shall not forget you, Tom," he said, smiling down into my eager face.
10094-8{_}1841{_}"I think it very likely that we shall march together to fight the French."
10094-8{_}1842{_}And those last words rang in my ears all the way back to Riverview.
10094-8{_}1843{_}CHAPTER XII  DOROTHY MAKES HER CHOICE   I had been much from home during the winter, and, engrossed in my own thoughts, had taken small account of what was passing, but I soon found enough to occupy me.
10094-8{_}1844{_}Dorothy had spent a month at Mount Pleasant, the seat of the Lees, some distance down the river, and when she returned, I soon began to suspect that she had left her heart there; for one day there came riding up to Riverview Mr. Willoughby Newton, whose estate was near Mount Pleasant, and the way that Dorothy blushed when she welcomed him aroused my ire at once.
10094-8{_}1845{_}Now Mr. Willoughby Newton was a very handsome and proper gentleman, and on his broad acres grew some of the sweetest tobacco that ever left Virginia; but I could scarce treat him civilly, which only shows what an insufferable puppy I still was, and I made myself most miserable.
10094-8{_}1846{_}His learning was more of the court and camp than of the bookshelf,--a defect which I soon discovered,--and I loved to set him tripping over some quibble of words, a proceeding which amused me vastly, though my mirth was shared by none of the others who witnessed it.
10094-8{_}1847{_}In fact, Madame Stewart was partial to the man from the first, in which I do not blame her, for a better match could not have been desired for her daughter.
10094-8{_}1848{_}She made him see his welcome, and he doubtless thought the road to Dorothy's heart a fair and easy one.
10094-8{_}1849{_}I certainly thought so, and I spent my days in moping about the place, cutting a most melancholy and unattractive figure.
10094-8{_}1850{_}I can look back now with a smile upon those days, realizing what a ridiculous sight I must have been, but at the time, their tragedy was for me a very real and living one.
10094-8{_}1851{_}Newton had passed some years in London, and had picked up there the graces of the court, as well as much of its frippery gossip, which latter he was fond of retailing, to my great disgust, but to the vast entertainment of the ladies, who found no fault with it, though it was four or five years old.
10094-8{_}1852{_}He could tell a story well and turn a joke to a nicety,--a fact which I was at that time far from admitting,--and under other circumstances I should have found him a witty and amusing friend.
10094-8{_}1853{_}I think he soon saw what my feelings were,--indeed, even a more obtuse man would have had no difficulty in understanding them,--and he treated me with a good-humored condescension which irritated me beyond measure.
10094-8{_}1854{_}And yet, unquestionably, it was the only treatment my behavior merited.
10094-8{_}1855{_}The climax came one evening after dinner.
10094-8{_}1856{_}We had both, perhaps, had a glass of wine too much before we joined the ladies.
10094-8{_}1857{_}Certainly, no words had passed between us when they had left the table, and there was nothing to do but drink, which we did with moody perseverance.
10094-8{_}1858{_}But once before the fire in the great hall, with Madame Stewart knitting on one side and Dorothy bending over her tambour on the other, his mood changed and he grew talkative enough, while I sat down near the candles and pretended to be absorbed in a book.
10094-8{_}1859{_}"Do you know, ladies," he said, "this reminds me of nothing so much as a night in London just five years ago, when the great earthquake was.
10094-8{_}1860{_}We were sitting around the fire, just as we are siting now, Tommy Collier on my right, and Harry Sibley on my left, when the bottles on the table began to clink and the windows to rattle, and poor Harry, who was leaning back in his chair, crashed over backwards to the floor.
10094-8{_}1861{_}We picked him up and went out into the street, where there was confusion worse confounded.
10094-8{_}1862{_}Windows were thrown open, women were running up and down clad only in their smocks, and one fellow had mounted a barrel and was calling on the people to repent because the Day of Judgment was at hand.
10094-8{_}1863{_}Somebody predicted there would be another earthquake in a week, and so the next day the people began to pour out of town, not because they were frightened, but 'Lord, the weather is so fine,' they said, 'one can't help going into the country.'"
10094-8{_}1864{_}"You found the country very pleasant, Mr. Newton, I dare say," I remarked, looking up from my book.
10094-8{_}1865{_}He did not at once understand the meaning of my question, but Dorothy did, and flushed crimson with anger.
10094-8{_}1866{_}The sight of her disapproval and Madame Stewart's frowning face maddened me.
10094-8{_}1867{_}"No," he said slowly, after a moment, "I did not leave the city, but hundreds of people did.
10094-8{_}1868{_}Within three days, over seven hundred coaches were counted passing Hyde Park corner, with whole families going to the country.
10094-8{_}1869{_}The clergy preached that it was judgment on London for its wickedness, and that the next earthquake would swallow up the whole town.
10094-8{_}1870{_}The ridotto had to be put off because there was no one to attend it, and the women who remained in town spent their time between reading Sherlock's sermons and making earthquake gowns, in which they proposed to sit out of doors all night."
10094-8{_}1871{_}"Pray, what was the color of your gown, Mr.
10094-8{_}1872{_}Newton?"
10094-8{_}1873{_}I inquired, with a polite show of interest.
10094-8{_}1874{_}Newton rose slowly from his chair and came toward me.
10094-8{_}1875{_}"Am I to understand that you mean to insult me, sir?"
10094-8{_}1876{_}he asked, when he had got quite near.
10094-8{_}1877{_}"You are to understand whatever you please," I answered hotly, throwing my book upon the table.
10094-8{_}1878{_}"Tom," cried Dorothy, "for shame, sir!
10094-8{_}1879{_}Have you taken leave of your senses?"
10094-8{_}1880{_}"Do not be frightened, I beg of you, Miss Randolph," interrupted Newton, restraining her with one hand.
10094-8{_}1881{_}"I assure you that I have no intention of injuring the boy."
10094-8{_}1882{_}"Injuring me, indeed!"
10094-8{_}1883{_}I cried, springing to my feet, furious with rage, for I could not bear to be patronized.
10094-8{_}1884{_}"It is you who are insulting, and by God you shall answer for it!"
10094-8{_}1885{_}"As you will," he said, with a light laugh, and turned back to the fire.
10094-8{_}1886{_}I knew that I had got all the worst of the encounter, that I had behaved with a rudeness for which there was no excuse, and that I cut a sorry figure standing there, and my face burned at the knowledge.
10094-8{_}1887{_}But preserving what semblance of dignity I could, I stalked from the hall and upstairs to my room.
10094-8{_}1888{_}I sat a long time thinking over the occurrence, and the more I pondered it, the more clearly I saw that I had played the fool.
10094-8{_}1889{_}I did not know then, but I learned long afterward, that my conduct that night came near losing me the great happiness of my life.
10094-8{_}1890{_}My cheeks flush even now as I think of my behavior.
10094-8{_}1891{_}How foolish do the tragedies of youth appear, once time has tamed the blood!
10094-8{_}1892{_}I did not wonder in the morning to receive a summons from my aunt, and I found her in her accustomed chair before the table piled with papers.
10094-8{_}1893{_}She glanced at me coldly as I entered, and finished looking over a paper she held in her hand before she spoke to me.
10094-8{_}1894{_}"I need not tell you," she said at length, "how greatly your boorish conduct of last night surprised me.
10094-8{_}1895{_}To insult a guest, and especially to do so without provocation, is not the part of a gentleman."
10094-8{_}1896{_}I flushed angrily, for the justness of this statement only irritated me the more.
10094-8{_}1897{_}I think it is always the man who is in the wrong that shows the greatest violence, and the man that most deserves rebuke who is most impatient of it.
10094-8{_}1898{_}"There is no need for you to counsel me how a gentleman should behave," I answered hotly.
10094-8{_}1899{_}"I did not summon you here to counsel you," she said still more coldly, "but to inform you that this disgraceful affair is to go no further, at least beneath this roof.
10094-8{_}1900{_}Mr. Newton has promised me to overlook your behavior, which is most generous on his part, and I trust you will see the wisdom of making peace with him."
10094-8{_}1901{_}"And why, may I ask, madame?"
10094-8{_}1902{_}"Because," she said, looking me in the eyes, "it is most likely that he will marry my daughter, and nothing is more vulgar than a family whose members are forever quarreling."
10094-8{_}1903{_}I clenched my hands until the nails pierced the flesh.
10094-8{_}1904{_}She had hit me a hard blow, and she knew it.
10094-8{_}1905{_}"And what does Dorothy think of this arrangement?"
10094-8{_}1906{_}I asked, with as great composure as I could muster.
10094-8{_}1907{_}She smiled with a calm assurance which made my heart sink.
10094-8{_}1908{_}"Dorothy would be a fool not to accept him, for he is one of the most eligible gentlemen in Virginia.
10094-8{_}1909{_}Indeed, perhaps she has already done so, for I gave him leave to speak to her this morning," and she smiled again as she noted my trembling hands, which I tried in vain to steady.
10094-8{_}1910{_}"You seem much interested in the matter."
10094-8{_}1911{_}I turned from her without replying,--I could trust myself no further.
10094-8{_}1912{_}Not that I blamed her for hating me,--for she loved her son and I was the shadow across his path,--but she was pressing me further than I had counted on.
10094-8{_}1913{_}I snatched up my hat as I ran along the hall and out the great door toward the river.
10094-8{_}1914{_}Spring was coming, the trees were shaking out their foliage, along the river the wild flowers were beginning to show their tiny faces, but I saw none of these as I broke my way through the brush along the water's edge,--for perhaps even now he was asking Dorothy to be his wife, and she was yielding to him.
10094-8{_}1915{_}The thought maddened me,--yet why should she do otherwise?
10094-8{_}1916{_}What claim had I upon her?
10094-8{_}1917{_}And yet I had builded such a different future for her and me.
10094-8{_}1918{_}I had walked I know not how long when I came out suddenly upon the road which wound along the bank and finally dipped to the ferry, and here I sat down upon a log to think.
10094-8{_}1919{_}If Dorothy accepted him, I could no longer stay at Riverview.
10094-8{_}1920{_}I must go away to Williamsburg and seek employment in the campaign, if only as a ranger.
10094-8{_}1921{_}It must soon commence, and surely they would not refuse me in the ranks.
10094-8{_}1922{_}As I sat absorbed in bitter thought, I heard the sound of hoof beats up the road and saw a horseman coming.
10094-8{_}1923{_}I drew back behind a tree, for I was in no mood to talk to any one, and gloomily watched him as he drew nearer.
10094-8{_}1924{_}There seemed something strangely familiar about the figure, and in an instant I recognized him.
10094-8{_}1925{_}It was Willoughby Newton.
10094-8{_}1926{_}In another moment he had passed, his face a picture of rage and shame.
10094-8{_}1927{_}He was riding away from Riverview in anger, and as I realized what that meant, I sprang forward with a great cry of joy.
10094-8{_}1928{_}He must have heard me, for he turned in the saddle and shook his whip at me, and for an instant drew rein as though to stop.
10094-8{_}1929{_}But he thought better of it, for he settled again in the saddle, and was soon out of sight down the road.
10094-8{_}1930{_}I had not waited so long, for settling my hat on my head, I set off up the road as fast as my legs would carry me.
10094-8{_}1931{_}It seemed to me I should never reach the house, and I cursed the folly which had taken me so far away, but at last I ran up the steps and into the hall.
10094-8{_}1932{_}As I entered, I caught a glimpse of a well-known gown in the hall above, and in an instant I was up the stairs.
