10094-8{_}3277{_}"Colonel Washington," he sneered, his lips curling away from his teeth with rage, "was, I believe, an aide on the general's staff.
10094-8{_}3278{_}Since the general is dead, that position no longer exists.
10094-8{_}3279{_}Consequently, Colonel Washington is no longer an officer of the army, and I fail to see what right he has to take part in this discussion."
10094-8{_}3280{_}Half a dozen of us were on our feet in an instant, but Washington was before us and waved us back with a motion of his hand.
10094-8{_}3281{_}"Colonel Innes is right," he said, his deep-set eyes gleaming like two coals of fire.
10094-8{_}3282{_}"I am no longer an officer of the army, and I thank God this is so, since it is about to further disgrace itself."
10094-8{_}3283{_}"Take care, sir," cried Innes, springing to his feet.
10094-8{_}3284{_}"You forget there is such a thing as court-martial."
10094-8{_}3285{_}"And you forget that I am no longer of the army, and so can defy its discipline."
10094-8{_}3286{_}He stood for a moment longer looking Innes in the eyes, and then, without saluting, turned on his heel and left the place.
10094-8{_}3287{_}A moment later the council broke up in confusion, for Innes saw plainly that the sentiment of nearly all the other officers present was against him, and he did not choose to give it opportunity of expression.
10094-8{_}3288{_}I had scarcely reached my quarters when I received a note from his secretary stating that as the mortality among the Virginia companies had been so heavy, it had been decided to unite the three into one, and my lieutenancy was therefore abolished.
10094-8{_}3289{_}Trembling with anger, I hurried to Washington's quarters and laid the note before him.
10094-8{_}3290{_}"Why, Tom," he said, with a short laugh, after he had read it, "we seem to have fallen into disgrace together.
10094-8{_}3291{_}But come," he added more cheerfully, seeing my downcast face, "do not despair.
10094-8{_}3292{_}We may yet win out.
10094-8{_}3293{_}The governor and the House of Burgesses will not receive so quietly this project to retire from the frontier.
10094-8{_}3294{_}I had a letter from Dinwiddie but the other day, in which he said as much.
10094-8{_}3295{_}In the mean time, I am going home to Mount Vernon to rest, and you must come with me."
10094-8{_}3296{_}I accepted readily enough, for I knew not what else to do, and on the morrow we set out.
10094-8{_}3297{_}Colonel Washington was so ill that we could proceed but slowly.
10094-8{_}3298{_}We finally reached Winchester, and from there, because of the better road, crossed the river to Frederick, where a great surprise awaited us.
10094-8{_}3299{_}For scarcely were we off our horses at the little tavern, than the host, learning our names, rushed away down the wide, rambling street, crying the news aloud, to our great wonderment, who saw not why it should interest any one.
10094-8{_}3300{_}In an incredibly short time, above a hundred people had gathered before the inn, cheering and hallooing with all their might, while we looked at them in dumb amazement.
10094-8{_}3301{_}We sent for the host to learn what this might mean, thinking doubtless there was some mistake, and even as he entered, a dozen men burst into the room, and insisted that we should not be permitted for a moment to think of putting up at an inn, but should accompany them home.
10094-8{_}3302{_}"But, gentlemen," protested Washington, "you have mistaken us for some one else.
10094-8{_}3303{_}We have done nothing to deserve your hospitality."
10094-8{_}3304{_}"Have you not?"
10094-8{_}3305{_}they cried, and they hustled us out into the yard.
10094-8{_}3306{_}There was no denying them, so off we rode again, greatly bewildered, and in the course of half an hour were being introduced by our self-appointed entertainer to his wife and three pretty daughters.
10094-8{_}3307{_}"'T is Colonel Washington, you understand, wife," he cried.
10094-8{_}3308{_}"Colonel Washington, whose advice, had it been followed, would have saved the expedition."
10094-8{_}3309{_}A great light broke upon me.
10094-8{_}3310{_}So my friend's merits were to be recognized at last,--were to win him something more than contumely and insult,--and as he would have made denial, I cut him short.
10094-8{_}3311{_}"Do not listen to him!"
10094-8{_}3312{_}I cried.
10094-8{_}3313{_}"'T is true, every word of it, and much more besides."
10094-8{_}3314{_}Whereat the girls smiled at me very sweetly, our host wrung my hand again, and I swear there were tears in Washington's eyes as he looked at me in feigned anger.
10094-8{_}3315{_}Such a night's entertainment as was given us I shall not soon forget, nor Colonel Washington either, I dare say.
10094-8{_}3316{_}Word of our presence had got about the neighborhood with singular speed, and the people flocked in by dozens, until the great hallway, which ran through the house from front to rear, was crowded from end to end.
10094-8{_}3317{_}Then, nothing would do but that Colonel Washington must tell the story of the advance, the ambuscade, and the retreat, which he did with such consummate slighting of his own part in the campaign that I interrupted him in great indignation, and, unheeding his protests, related some of the things concerning him which I have already written, and which, I swear, were very well received.
10094-8{_}3318{_}"But Lieutenant Stewart says nothing of what he himself did," cried Washington, when I had finished.
10094-8{_}3319{_}"Because I did nothing worth relating," I retorted, my cheeks hot with embarrassment at the way they looked at me.
10094-8{_}3320{_}"Ask him how he won that sword he wears at his side," he continued, not heeding my interruption, his eyes twinkling at my discomfiture.
10094-8{_}3321{_}"Believe me, 'tis not many Virginia officers can boast such a fine one."
10094-8{_}3322{_}And then, of course, they all demanded that he tell the story, which he did with an exaggeration that I considered little less than shameful.
10094-8{_}3323{_}In some mysterious manner, tankards of cold, bitter Dutch beer, the kind that is so refreshing after a journey or at the close of a hot day's work, had found their way into the right hand of every man present, and as Washington ended the story and I was yet denying, our host sprang to his feet.
10094-8{_}3324{_}"We'll drink to the troops of Maryland and Virginia," he cried, "who behaved like soldiers and died like men, teaching England's redcoats a lesson they will not soon forget, and to two of the bravest among them, Colonel Washington and Lieutenant Stewart!"
10094-8{_}3325{_}It was done with a cheer that made the old hall ring, and when, half an hour later, I found myself beside the prettiest of the three daughters of the house, I was not yet quite recovered.
10094-8{_}3326{_}Only this I can say,--it is a pleasant thing to be a hero, though trying to the nerves.
10094-8{_}3327{_}I had only the one experience, and did not merit that, as the reader has doubtless decided for himself.
10094-8{_}3328{_}Of course there was a dance,--what merrymaking would be complete without one?--and Colonel Washington walked a minuet with a certain Mistress Patience Burd, with a grace which excited the admiration of every swain in the room, and the envy of not a few,--myself among the number, for I was ever but a clumsy dancer, and on this occasion no doubt greatly vexed my pretty partner.
10094-8{_}3329{_}But every night must end, as this one did at last.
10094-8{_}3330{_}Colonel Washington was much better next morning, for his illness had been more of the mind than of the body, and our kind reception had done wonders to banish his vexation.
10094-8{_}3331{_}Our friends bade us Godspeed, and we rode on our way southward.
10094-8{_}3332{_}I never saw the house again, and it is one of my great regrets and reasons for self-reproach that I have forgot the name of the honest man who was our host that night, and remember only that the name of his prettiest daughter was Betty.
10094-8{_}3333{_}As we reached a part of the country which was more closely settled, I soon perceived that however great dishonor had accrued to British arms and British reputations as the result of that battle by the Monongahela, Colonel Washington had won only respect and admiration by his consistent and courageous conduct.
10094-8{_}3334{_}We were stopped a hundred times by people who asked first for news, and when they heard my companion's name, vied with one another to do him honor.
10094-8{_}3335{_}It did me good to see how he brightened under these kind words and friendly acts, and how the color came again into his face and the light into his eyes.
10094-8{_}3336{_}And I hold that this was as it should be, for I know of nothing of which a man may be more justly proud than of the well-earned praises of his fellows.
10094-8{_}3337{_}At last, toward the evening of a sultry August day, we turned our horses' heads into the wide road which led up to Mount Vernon, and drew near to that hospitable and familiar mansion.
10094-8{_}3338{_}News of our approach must have preceded us, for there, drawn up in line, were the bowing and grinning negroes, while at the entrance gate were Mrs. Washington and her children, as well as a dozen families assembled from as many miles around to do honor to the returning warrior.
10094-8{_}3339{_}My heart beat more quickly as I ran my eyes over this gathering, but fell again when I saw that the family from Riverview was not there.
10094-8{_}3340{_}And such a greeting as it was!
10094-8{_}3341{_}We all remained a space apart until Mrs. Washington had kissed her son, as something too sacred for our intrusion.
10094-8{_}3342{_}But when he turned to greet his neighbors, I have rarely seen such genuine emotion shown even in our whole-hearted Virginia.
10094-8{_}3343{_}At the great dinner which followed, with Mrs. Washington at the head of the table and her son at the foot, we told again the story of the campaign, and the men forgot to sip their wine until the tale was ended.
10094-8{_}3344{_}Yet with all this largess of goodwill, I was not wholly happy.
10094-8{_}3345{_}For I had no home to go to, nor was there any waiting to welcome me, and the woman I loved seemed farther away than ever, though now she was so near.
10094-8{_}3346{_}CHAPTER XXII  A NEW DANGER AT RIVERVIEW   But Dorothy was not so near as I had thought, for next morning came a message from my aunt.
10094-8{_}3347{_}It was delivered almost as soon as I was out of bed by a negro boy who had ridden over at daybreak.
10094-8{_}3348{_}It was dated but two days before, and began very formally.
10094-8{_}3349{_}"Sir," it ran, "since you no doubt will wish to recuperate from the fatigues of the campaign so unfortunately ended, and as there is no place where you can do this so well as at Riverview, I hasten to assure you that the place is entirely at your service."
10094-8{_}3350{_}I paused a moment to get my breath.
10094-8{_}3351{_}Her reference to the campaign was intended as a stab, of course, yet could it be she was relenting?
10094-8{_}3352{_}But hope fell as I read on.
10094-8{_}3353{_}"In order that you may feel at liberty to avail yourself of this invitation," the note continued, "my daughter and I have accepted one of long standing to spend a month, or perhaps two months, at the home of a relative.
10094-8{_}3354{_}James is at Williamsburg, so that you may be entirely free to occupy your leisure at Riverview as best pleases you.
10094-8{_}3355{_}Do not think that you have driven us from the place, for that is not at all the case.
10094-8{_}3356{_}I have long felt the need of rest, and take advantage of this opportunity, while there is little doing on the plantation, to secure it.
10094-8{_}3357{_}I trust to your sense of honor to make no inquiries as to where we are stopping, nor to attempt to see my daughter, who, I believe, has already discovered that any fancy she may ever have seemed to entertain for you was more imaginary than real."
10094-8{_}3358{_}Here was a blow, straight from the shoulder, and I winced under it.
10094-8{_}3359{_}"I could never consent," the note concluded, "to any attachment of a serious nature between you, having quite other views for my daughter, which, I am sure, will be for her happiness and well-being."
10094-8{_}3360{_}I read the note through a second time before I realized what a blow it gave to all my hopes.
10094-8{_}3361{_}I had had little cause to anticipate any other treatment, it is true, and yet I have often observed that men hope most who have least reason for it, and this was so in my case.
10094-8{_}3362{_}As I read the note again, I could not but admire the adroitness of its author.
10094-8{_}3363{_}She had placed me upon honor--without my consent, 't is true--to make no effort to see Dorothy.
10094-8{_}3364{_}I stood biting my lips with anger and vexation, and then, with sudden resolve, turned back to the messenger.
10094-8{_}3365{_}"Go around to the kitchen and get something to eat, if you are hungry," I said to him.
10094-8{_}3366{_}"I shall be ready to ride back with you in half an hour;" and as he disappeared around a corner of the house, agrin from ear to ear at the prospect of refreshment, I sought Mrs. Washington and told her that I had just received a note from my aunt and would ride to Riverview at once.
10094-8{_}3367{_}How much she suspected of my difference with my aunt, I do not know, but if she experienced any surprise at my sudden departure, she certainly did not show it, saying only that she regretted that I must go so soon, and that I must always consider Mount Vernon no less my home than Riverview,--an assurance which Colonel Washington repeated when the moment came to say good-by, and I rode away at last with a very tender feeling in my heart for those two figures which stood there on the steps until I turned into the road and passed from sight.
10094-8{_}3368{_}"And how is everything at Riverview, Sam?"
10094-8{_}3369{_}I asked of the boy, as we struck into the road and settled our horses into an easy canter.
10094-8{_}3370{_}He did not answer for a moment, and when I glanced at him to see the cause of his silence, I was astonished to find him rolling his eyes about as though he saw a ghost.
10094-8{_}3371{_}"What's the matter, boy?"
10094-8{_}3372{_}I asked sharply.
10094-8{_}3373{_}"Come, speak out.
10094-8{_}3374{_}What is it?"
10094-8{_}3375{_}He looked behind him and all around into the woods, and then urged his horse close to mine.
10094-8{_}3376{_}"Mas' Tom," he said, almost in a whisper, "dere's gwine t' be hell at d' plantation foh long.
10094-8{_}3377{_}Youse stay 'way fum it."
10094-8{_}3378{_}I looked at him, still more astonished by his singular behavior.
10094-8{_}3379{_}A full-blooded negro does not turn pale, but under the influence of great terror his skin grows spotted and livid.
10094-8{_}3380{_}Sam's was livid at that moment.
10094-8{_}3381{_}"See here, Sam," I said sharply, "if you have anything to tell, I want you to tell me right away.
10094-8{_}3382{_}What are you afraid of?"
10094-8{_}3383{_}"D' witch man," he whispered, his eyes almost starting from his head, and his forehead suddenly beading with perspiration.
10094-8{_}3384{_}"The witch man?
10094-8{_}3385{_}Has a witch man come to Riverview?"
10094-8{_}3386{_}He nodded.
10094-8{_}3387{_}"And what is he doing there, Sam?"
10094-8{_}3388{_}"He says d' French dun whopped d' English, an' a-comin' t' set all d' niggahs free.
10094-8{_}3389{_}He says we mus' holp, an' dere won't be no mo' slaves.
10094-8{_}3390{_}All ub us be free, jus' like white folks."
10094-8{_}3391{_}It took me a minute or two to grasp the full meaning of this extraordinary revelation.
10094-8{_}3392{_}"He says the French are coming to set all the niggers free?"
10094-8{_}3393{_}I repeated.
10094-8{_}3394{_}Sam nodded.
10094-8{_}3395{_}"And that the niggers must help them?"
10094-8{_}3396{_}Again Sam nodded.
10094-8{_}3397{_}"Help them how, Sam?"
10094-8{_}3398{_}He hesitated.
10094-8{_}3399{_}"By killing the English, Sam?"
10094-8{_}3400{_}"I reckon dat 's it," he said reluctantly.
10094-8{_}3401{_}"And burning down their houses, perhaps?"
10094-8{_}3402{_}"I 'se hearn dat talked erboat, too."
10094-8{_}3403{_}I drew my horse in with a jerk, and catching Sam's by the bridle, pulled it to me.
10094-8{_}3404{_}"Now, boy," I said, "you must tell me all about this.
10094-8{_}3405{_}I promise you that no one shall harm you."
10094-8{_}3406{_}He began to whimper.
10094-8{_}3407{_}"I'll tell yo', Mas' Tom," he stuttered, "but yo' mus' n' hurt d' witch man."
10094-8{_}3408{_}"Who is this witch man?"
10094-8{_}3409{_}I demanded.
10094-8{_}3410{_}"Ole uncle Polete."
10094-8{_}3411{_}"Polete's no witch man.
10094-8{_}3412{_}Why, Sam, you 've known him all your life.
10094-8{_}3413{_}He's nothing but an ordinary old nigger.
10094-8{_}3414{_}He's been on the plantation twenty or thirty years.
10094-8{_}3415{_}All that he needs is a good whipping."
10094-8{_}3416{_}But the boy only shook his head and sobbed the more.
10094-8{_}3417{_}"Ef he's a-killed," he cried, "his ha'nt 'll come back fo' me."
10094-8{_}3418{_}I saw in a moment what the boy was afraid of.
10094-8{_}3419{_}It was not of old Polete in the flesh, but in the spirit.
10094-8{_}3420{_}I thought for a moment.
10094-8{_}3421{_}Well, I had no reason to wish Polete any harm, yet if it were discovered that he had been inciting the slaves to insurrection, there was no power in the colony could save his life.
10094-8{_}3422{_}If his owner did not execute him, the governor would take the matter out of his hands, and order it done himself.
10094-8{_}3423{_}"I tell you what I'll do, Sam," I said at last.
10094-8{_}3424{_}"You tell me everything you know, and I'll do all I can to save Polete.
10094-8{_}3425{_}I believe I can stop this thing without calling in any outside help."
10094-8{_}3426{_}He agreed to this, and as we jogged along I gradually drew the details of the plot from him.
10094-8{_}3427{_}The news of our defeat had, it seemed, stirred up the negroes at the plantation, and in some way the wild rumor had been started that a great force of French was marching over the mountains to conquer Virginia and all the other English colonies; that emissaries had come to the negroes and promised them that if they would assist the invading army, they would be given their freedom and half of the colony to live in.
10094-8{_}3428{_}It was at this time that old Polete, crazed, perhaps, by working in the tobacco fields under the blazing sun, had suddenly developed into a witch man, and proclaimed that he could see the French army marching, and urged the negroes to strike a blow at once in order to merit their freedom when the French should come.
10094-8{_}3429{_}Meetings were held almost nightly in the woods some miles from their cabins, whence they stole away after dark by twos and threes.
10094-8{_}3430{_}Just what their plans were Sam did not know, as he did not belong to the inner council, but he believed that something would happen soon because of the increasing excitement of the older negroes who were acquainted with the plans.
10094-8{_}3431{_}I rode on for some time in silence, thinking over this story and trying to decide what I would better do.
10094-8{_}3432{_}I did not know until months later that signs of unrest had been observed among the slaves all over the colony, and that the governor had considered the situation so serious that he had sent out many warnings concerning the danger.
10094-8{_}3433{_}It was as well, perhaps, that I did not know this then, for I might not have thought my own portion of the problem so easy of solution.
10094-8{_}3434{_}At the time, I had no thought but that the outbreak was the result of old Polete's prophecies, and was confined alone to Riverview.
10094-8{_}3435{_}Sam was cantering along behind me, his face still livid with terror, and as I caught sight of it again, I wondered what impulse it was had moved him to confide in me, with such fancied peril to himself.
10094-8{_}3436{_}"I would n' tole nobody else," he said, in answer to my question, "but you tole a lie fo' me oncet, an' saved me a lickin'."
10094-8{_}3437{_}"Told a lie for you, Sam?"
10094-8{_}3438{_}I questioned in astonishment.
10094-8{_}3439{_}"When was that?"
10094-8{_}3440{_}"Don' yo' 'membah boat d' whip, Mas' Tom, what I stole?"
10094-8{_}3441{_}he asked.
10094-8{_}3442{_}I looked at him for a moment before that incident of my boyhood came back to me.
10094-8{_}3443{_}"Why, yes, I remember it now," I said.
10094-8{_}3444{_}"But that was years ago, Sam, and I had forgotten it.
10094-8{_}3445{_}Besides, I didn't tell a lie for you.
10094-8{_}3446{_}I only told old Gump that I wished to give you the whip."
10094-8{_}3447{_}"Well," said Sam, looking at me doubtfully, "yo' saved me a lickin' anyhow, an' I did n' f 'git it," and he dropped back again.
10094-8{_}3448{_}Well, to be sure, an act of thoughtfulness or mercy never hurts a man, a fact which I have since learned for myself a hundred times, and wish all men realized.
10094-8{_}3449{_}We were soon at Riverview, and I ordered Sam to ride out to the field where the men were working, and tell the overseer, Long, that I wished to see him.
10094-8{_}3450{_}Sam departed on the errand, visibly uneasy, and I wandered from my room, where I had taken my pack, along the hall and into my aunt's business room while I waited his return.
10094-8{_}3451{_}I stood again for a moment at the spot on the staircase where I had kissed Dorothy that morning,--it seemed ages ago,--and as I looked up, I fancied I could still see her sweet face gazing down at me.
10094-8{_}3452{_}But it was only fancy, and, with a sigh, I turned away and went down through the hall.
10094-8{_}3453{_}There were reminders of her at every turn,--there was the place where she had sat sewing in the evenings; over the fireplace hung a little picture she had painted, rude enough, no doubt, but beautiful to my eyes.
10094-8{_}3454{_}With a sudden impulse, I ran down the steps and to the old seat under the oaks by the river.
10094-8{_}3455{_}Nothing had changed,--even the shadows across the water seemed to be the same.
10094-8{_}3456{_}But as I ran my hand mechanically along the arm of the seat on the side where Dorothy always sat my fingers felt a roughness which had not been there before, and as I looked to see what this might be, I saw that some one had cut in the wood a T and a D, intertwined, and circled by a tiny heart.
10094-8{_}3457{_}Who could have done it?
10094-8{_}3458{_}I had no need to ask myself the question.
10094-8{_}3459{_}My heart told me that no one but Dorothy could have done it, and that she knew that I should come and sit here and live over again the long evenings when she had sat beside me.
10094-8{_}3460{_}It was a message from my love, and with trembling lips I bent and kissed the letters which she had carved.
10094-8{_}3461{_}As I sat erect again, I heard footsteps behind me, and turned to see Long approaching.
10094-8{_}3462{_}"You sent for me, Mr.
10094-8{_}3463{_}Stewart?"
10094-8{_}3464{_}he asked.
10094-8{_}3465{_}"I saw you sitting here, and decided you were waiting for me."
10094-8{_}3466{_}"Yes," I said, and I shook hands with him, for he was an honest man and a good workman.
10094-8{_}3467{_}"I am glad to see you back again, sir, though looking so ill," he added.
10094-8{_}3468{_}"I trust the air of Riverview will soon bring you around all right," and from his eyes I knew he meant it.
10094-8{_}3469{_}I thanked him, and bade him sit beside me.
10094-8{_}3470{_}Then, in a few words, I told him what I had learned of the negro meetings, and saw his face grow grave.
10094-8{_}3471{_}"'Tis what I have always feared," he said, when I had finished.
10094-8{_}3472{_}"There are too many of them in the colony, and they feel their strength.
10094-8{_}3473{_}If they had a leader and a chance to combine, they might do a great deal of harm.
10094-8{_}3474{_}However, we shall soon knock this in the head."
10094-8{_}3475{_}"How?"
10094-8{_}3476{_}I asked.
10094-8{_}3477{_}"Make an example of Polete," he answered decidedly.
10094-8{_}3478{_}"That's the best way, sir.
10094-8{_}3479{_}Put him out of the way, let the other niggers see us do it, and they'll quiet down fast enough."
10094-8{_}3480{_}"Undoubtedly that is the easiest way," I said, smiling, "but, unfortunately, I had to promise the person who gave me the information that Polete should not be harmed."
10094-8{_}3481{_}Long stared at me for a moment in amazement.
10094-8{_}3482{_}"It would be unfortunate if any of the other planters should hear of that promise, Mr. Stewart," he said at last.
10094-8{_}3483{_}"They would probably take Polete's case into their own hands."
10094-8{_}3484{_}I laughed at his evident concern.
10094-8{_}3485{_}"No doubt," I said, "but they are not going to hear of it.
10094-8{_}3486{_}I intend telling no one but yourself, for we two are quite sufficient to stop this thing right here, and it need go no further."
10094-8{_}3487{_}"Perhaps we are," he answered doubtfully.
10094-8{_}3488{_}"What is your plan, sir?"
10094-8{_}3489{_}"Polete will hold a meeting to-night over there in the woods.
10094-8{_}3490{_}Well, we will be present at the meeting."
10094-8{_}3491{_}He looked at me without saying a word.
10094-8{_}3492{_}"Our visit will probably not be very welcome," I continued, "but I believe it will produce the desired effect.
10094-8{_}3493{_}Will you go with me?"
10094-8{_}3494{_}"Certainly," he answered readily, "but I still think my plan the best, sir."
10094-8{_}3495{_}"Perhaps it is," I laughed, "but we will try mine first," and he went back to the field, agreeing to be at the house at eight o'clock.
10094-8{_}3496{_}I covered with my hand the tiny letters on the arm of the bench, and, looking out across the broad river, drifted into the land of dreams, where Dorothy and I wandered together along a primrose path, with none to interfere.
10094-8{_}3497{_}CHAPTER XXIII  THE GOVERNOR SHOWS HIS GRATITUDE   I ate my supper in solitary splendor in the old dining-room, with my grandfather's portrait looking down upon me, and Long found me an hour later sitting in the midst of a wreath of smoke just within the hallway out of the river mist.
10094-8{_}3498{_}"'T was as you said, Mr. Stewart," he remarked, as he joined me.
10094-8{_}3499{_}"Fully a hundred of the niggers stole off to the woods to-night so soon as it was dark.
10094-8{_}3500{_}They went down toward the old Black Snake swamp."
10094-8{_}3501{_}"Very well," I said, rising.
10094-8{_}3502{_}"Wait till I get my hat, and I am with you."
10094-8{_}3503{_}"But you will go armed?"
10094-8{_}3504{_}he asked anxiously.
10094-8{_}3505{_}I paused to think for a moment.
10094-8{_}3506{_}"No, I will not," I said finally.
10094-8{_}3507{_}"A brace of pistols would avail nothing against that mob, should they choose to resist us, and our going unarmed will have a great moral effect upon them as showing them that we are not afraid."
10094-8{_}3508{_}"You have weighed fully the extent of the risk you are about to run, I hope, sir," protested Long.
10094-8{_}3509{_}"Fully," I answered.
10094-8{_}3510{_}"'T is not yet too late for you to turn back, you know.
10094-8{_}3511{_}I have no right to ask you to endanger your life to carry out this plan of mine.
10094-8{_}3512{_}Perhaps it would be wiser for you not to go."
10094-8{_}3513{_}"And if I stay, you"--  "Will go alone," I said.
10094-8{_}3514{_}He caught my hand and wrung it heartily.
10094-8{_}3515{_}"You are a brave man, Mr. Stewart," he exclaimed.
10094-8{_}3516{_}"If I have shown any hesitation, 't was on your account, not on my own.
10094-8{_}3517{_}I am ready to go with you," and as he spoke, he drew a brace of pistols from beneath his coat and laid them on the table by the fireplace.
10094-8{_}3518{_}"Wait one moment," I said, and hurrying to my aunt's room, I wrote a short note telling her of the trouble I had discovered and where Long and I were going, so that, if we did not return, she would know what had happened.
10094-8{_}3519{_}Folding and sealing it, I wrote on the outside, "To be delivered at once to Mrs. Stewart," left it on the table, knowing that no one would enter the room till morning, and hurried back to rejoin Long.
10094-8{_}3520{_}We were off without further words, and were soon well on our way.
10094-8{_}3521{_}It was a clear, cool, summer night, with the breeze just stirring in the trees and keeping up a faint, unceasing whispering among the leaves.
10094-8{_}3522{_}The moon had risen some hours before, and sailed upward through a cloudless sky.
10094-8{_}3523{_}Even under the trees it was not wholly dark, for the moon's light filtered through here and there, making a quaint patchwork on the ground, and filling the air with a peculiar iridescence which transformed the ragged trunks of the sycamores into fantastic hobgoblins.
10094-8{_}3524{_}All about us rose the croaking of the frogs, dominating all the other noises of the night, and uniting in one mighty chorus in the marshes along the river.
10094-8{_}3525{_}An owl was hooting from a distant tree, and the hum of innumerable insects sounded on every side.
10094-8{_}3526{_}Here and there a glittering, dew-spangled cobweb stretched across our path, a barrier of silver, and required more than ordinary resolution to be brushed aside.
10094-8{_}3527{_}As we turned nearer to the river, the ground grew softer and the underbrush more thick, and I knew that we had reached the swamp.
10094-8{_}3528{_}Then, in a moment, it seemed to me that I could hear some faint, monotonous singsong rising above all the rest.
10094-8{_}3529{_}At first I thought it was the croaking of a monster frog, but as we plodded on and the sound grew more distinct, I knew it could not be that.
10094-8{_}3530{_}At last, in sheer perplexity, I stopped and motioned Long to listen.
10094-8{_}3531{_}"Do you hear it?"
10094-8{_}3532{_}I asked.
10094-8{_}3533{_}"Do you know what it is?"
10094-8{_}3534{_}"Yes, I have heard it for the last ten minutes, Mr. Stewart," he answered quietly.
10094-8{_}3535{_}"It is old Polete preaching to the niggers.
10094-8{_}3536{_}I have often heard their so-called witch men preach.
10094-8{_}3537{_}It is always in a singsong just like that."
10094-8{_}3538{_}As we drew nearer, I perceived that this was true, for I could catch the tones of the speaker's voice, and in a few minutes could distinguish his words.
10094-8{_}3539{_}Some years before, when the river had been in flood, its current had been thrown against this bank by a landslide on the other side, and had washed away trees and underbrush for some distance.
10094-8{_}3540{_}The underbrush had soon sprung up again, but the clearing still remained, and as we stopped in the shadow of the trees and looked across it, we saw a singular sight.
10094-8{_}3541{_}Negroes to the number of at least a hundred and fifty were gathered about a pile of logs on which Polete was mounted.
10094-8{_}3542{_}He was shouting in a monotone, his voice rising and falling in regular cadence, his eyes closed, his head tilted back, his face turned toward the moon, whose light silvered his hair and beard and gave a certain majesty to his appearance.
10094-8{_}3543{_}His hearers were seemingly much affected, and interrupted him from time to time with shouts and groans and loud amens.
10094-8{_}3544{_}"Dis is d' promise' lan'!"
10094-8{_}3545{_}cried old Polete, waving his arms above his head in a wild ecstasy.
10094-8{_}3546{_}"All we hab t' do is t' raise up an' take it from ouh 'pressahs.
10094-8{_}3547{_}Ef we stays hyah slaves, it's ouh own fault.
10094-8{_}3548{_}Now's d' 'pinted time.
10094-8{_}3549{_}D' French is ma'chin' obah d' mountings t' holp us.
10094-8{_}3550{_}Dee'll drib d' English into d' sea, and wese t' hab ouh freedom,--ouh freedom an' plenty lan' t' lib on."
10094-8{_}3551{_}"Dat's it," shouted some one, "an' we gwine t' holp, suah!"
10094-8{_}3552{_}The negroes were so intent upon their speaker that they did not perceive us until we were right among them, and even then for a few minutes, as we forced our way through the mob, no one knew us.
10094-8{_}3553{_}"It's Mas' Tom!"
10094-8{_}3554{_}yelled one big fellow, as my hat was knocked from my head.
10094-8{_}3555{_}And, as if by instinct, they crowded back on either side, and a path was opened before us to the pile of logs where Polete stood.
10094-8{_}3556{_}He gaped at us amazedly as we clambered up toward him, and I saw that he was licking his lips convulsively.
10094-8{_}3557{_}A yell from the crowd greeted us as we appeared beside him,--a menacing yell, which died away into a low growling, and foretold an approaching storm.
10094-8{_}3558{_}"Now, boys," I cried, "I want you to listen to me for a minute.
10094-8{_}3559{_}That is a lie about the French coming over the mountains,--every word of it.
10094-8{_}3560{_}If Polete here, who, you know, is only a laborer like most of you, says he has seen them coming in a vision, why he's simply lying to you, or he doesn't know what he's talking about.
10094-8{_}3561{_}There are not three hundred Frenchmen the other side of the mountains, in the first place, and it will be winter before they can get any more there.
10094-8{_}3562{_}So if you fight, you will have to fight alone, and you can guess how much chance of success you have.
10094-8{_}3563{_}You know the penalty for insurrection.
10094-8{_}3564{_}It's death, and not an easy death, either,--death by fire!
10094-8{_}3565{_}If you go ahead with this thing, no power on earth can save every one of you from the stake."
10094-8{_}3566{_}"It's a lie!"
10094-8{_}3567{_}yelled Polete.
10094-8{_}3568{_}"I did hab d' vision.
10094-8{_}3569{_}I did see d' French a-comin'--millions o' dem--all a-ma'chin' t'rough d' forest.
10094-8{_}3570{_}Dee's almost hyah.
10094-8{_}3571{_}Dee want us t' holp."
10094-8{_}3572{_}A hoarse yell interrupted him, and I saw that something must be done.
10094-8{_}3573{_}"Wait a minute, boys," I cried.
10094-8{_}3574{_}"Let me ask Polete a question.
10094-8{_}3575{_}You say you have seen the French marching, Polete?"
10094-8{_}3576{_}He nodded sullenly.
10094-8{_}3577{_}"What was the color of their uniforms?"
10094-8{_}3578{_}He hesitated a moment, but saw he must answer.
10094-8{_}3579{_}"Dee was all colors," he said.
10094-8{_}3580{_}"Red, blue, green,--all colors."
10094-8{_}3581{_}I saw that my moment of triumph was at hand.
10094-8{_}3582{_}"Now, boys," I cried, holding up my hand so that all might be quiet and hear my words.
10094-8{_}3583{_}"You may guess how much value there is in Polete's visions.
10094-8{_}3584{_}He says he has seen the French army marching, and he has just told me that their uniforms are all colors,--red, blue, green, and so on.
10094-8{_}3585{_}Now, if he has seen the army, he ought to know the color of the uniforms, ought he not?"
10094-8{_}3586{_}"Yes, yes," yelled the mob.
10094-8{_}3587{_}"Well, boys," I continued, "the French wear only one color uniform, and that color is just the one which Polete has not mentioned--white.
10094-8{_}3588{_}No Frenchman goes to war except in a white uniform."
10094-8{_}3589{_}They were all silent for a moment, and I saw them eyeing Polete distrustfully.
10094-8{_}3590{_}But he was foaming at the mouth with fury.
10094-8{_}3591{_}"A lie!"
10094-8{_}3592{_}he screamed.
10094-8{_}3593{_}"A lie, same's de uddah.
10094-8{_}3594{_}Don' yo' see what we mus' do?
10094-8{_}3595{_}Kill 'em!
10094-8{_}3596{_}Kill 'em, an' nobody else'll evah know!"
10094-8{_}3597{_}That low growling which I had heard before again ran through the crowd.
10094-8{_}3598{_}I must play my last card.
10094-8{_}3599{_}"You fools!"
10094-8{_}3600{_}I cried, "do you suppose we are the only ones who know?
10094-8{_}3601{_}If so much as a hair of our heads is touched, if we are not back among our friends safe and sound when morning comes, every dog among you will yelp his life out with a circle of fire about him!"
10094-8{_}3602{_}They were whining now, and I knew I had them conquered.
10094-8{_}3603{_}"I came here to-night to save you," I went on, after a moment.
10094-8{_}3604{_}"Return now quietly to your quarters, and nothing more will be said about this gathering.
10094-8{_}3605{_}Put out of your minds once for all the hope that the French will help you, for it is a lie.
10094-8{_}3606{_}And let this be the last time you hold a meeting here, or I will not answer for the consequences."
10094-8{_}3607{_}I waved them away with my hand, and they slunk off by twos and threes until all of them had disappeared in the shadow of the wood.
10094-8{_}3608{_}"And now, what shall we do with this cur?"
10094-8{_}3609{_}asked Long, in a low voice, at my elbow.
10094-8{_}3610{_}I turned and saw that he had old Polete gripped by the collar.
10094-8{_}3611{_}"He tried to run away," he added, "but I thought you might have something to say to him."
10094-8{_}3612{_}Polete was as near collapse as a man could be and yet be conscious.
10094-8{_}3613{_}He was trembling like a leaf, his eyes were bloodshot, and his lower jaw was working convulsively.
10094-8{_}3614{_}He turned an imploring gaze on me, and tried to speak, but could not.
10094-8{_}3615{_}"Polete," I said sternly, "I suppose you know that if this night's work gets out, as it is certain to do sooner or later, no power on earth can save your life?"
10094-8{_}3616{_}"Yes, massa," he muttered, and looked about him wildly, as though he already saw the flames at his feet.
10094-8{_}3617{_}"Well, Polete," I went on, "after the way you have acted to-night, I see no reason why I should try to save you.
10094-8{_}3618{_}You certainly did all you could to get me killed."
10094-8{_}3619{_}"Yes, massa," he said again, and would have fallen had not Long held him upright by the collar.
10094-8{_}3620{_}I waited a moment, for I thought he was going to faint, but he opened his eyes again and fixed them on me.
10094-8{_}3621{_}"Now listen," I went on, when he appeared able to understand me.
10094-8{_}3622{_}"I'm not going to kill you.
10094-8{_}3623{_}I'm going to give you a chance for your life,--not a very big chance, perhaps, but a great deal better one than you would have here."
10094-8{_}3624{_}"Yes, massa," he said a third time, and there was a gleam of hope in his face.
10094-8{_}3625{_}"I'm going to let you go," I concluded.
10094-8{_}3626{_}"I'd advise you to follow the river till you get beyond the settlements, and then try for Pennsylvania.
10094-8{_}3627{_}I promise you there'll be no pursuit, but if you ever show your face around here again, you're as good as dead."
10094-8{_}3628{_}Before I had finished, he had fallen to his knees and bowed his head upon my feet, with a peculiar reverence,--a relic, I suppose, of his life in Africa.
10094-8{_}3629{_}He was blubbering like a baby when he looked up at me.
10094-8{_}3630{_}"I'll nevah f'git yeh, Mas' Tom," he said.
10094-8{_}3631{_}"I'll nevah f'git yeh."
10094-8{_}3632{_}"That'll do, uncle," and I caught him by the collar and pulled him to his feet.
10094-8{_}3633{_}"I don't want to see you killed, but you'd better get away from here as fast as you can, and drop this witch man business for good and all.
10094-8{_}3634{_}Here's two shillings.
10094-8{_}3635{_}They'll get you something to eat when you get to Pennsylvania, but you'd better skirmish along in the woods the best you can till then, or you'll be jerked up for a runaway."
10094-8{_}3636{_}He murmured some inarticulate words,--of gratitude, perhaps,--and slid down from the pile of logs.
10094-8{_}3637{_}We watched him until he plunged into the woods to the south of the clearing, and then started back toward the house.
10094-8{_}3638{_}I was busy with my own thoughts as we went, and Long was also silent, so that scarcely a word passed between us until we reached the steps.
10094-8{_}3639{_}"Sit down a minute, Long," I said, as he started back to his quarters.
10094-8{_}3640{_}"I don't believe we'll have any more trouble with those fellows, but perhaps it would be well to watch them."
10094-8{_}3641{_}"Trust me for that, sir," he answered.
10094-8{_}3642{_}"I'll see to it that there are no more meetings of that kind.
10094-8{_}3643{_}With Polete away, there is little danger.
10094-8{_}3644{_}The only question is whether he will stay away."
10094-8{_}3645{_}"I think he will," and I looked out over the river thoughtfully.
10094-8{_}3646{_}"He seemed to understand the danger he was in.
10094-8{_}3647{_}If he returns, you will have to deliver him up to the authorities at once, of course."
10094-8{_}3648{_}"Well," said Long, "I'm not a bloodthirsty man, sir, as perhaps you know, but I think we'd be safer if he were dead.
10094-8{_}3649{_}Still, we'll be safe enough anyway, now the niggers know their plot is discovered.
10094-8{_}3650{_}But we were in a ticklish place there for a while this evening."
10094-8{_}3651{_}"Yes," I answered, with a smile.
10094-8{_}3652{_}"It was not so easy as I had expected.
10094-8{_}3653{_}I want to thank you, Long, for going with me.
10094-8{_}3654{_}It was a service on your part which showed you have the interest of the place at heart, and are not afraid of danger."
10094-8{_}3655{_}"That's all right, sir," he said awkwardly.
10094-8{_}3656{_}"Good-night."
10094-8{_}3657{_}"Wait till I get your pistols," I said.
10094-8{_}3658{_}"You left them in the hall, you know."
10094-8{_}3659{_}The moonlight was streaming through the open window, and as I stepped into the hall, I rubbed my eyes, for I thought I must be dreaming.
10094-8{_}3660{_}There in a great chair before the fireplace sat Colonel Washington.
10094-8{_}3661{_}His head had fallen back, his eyes were closed, and from his deep and regular breathing I knew that he was sleeping.
10094-8{_}3662{_}Marveling greatly at his presence here at this hour, I tiptoed around him, got Long's pistols, and took them out to him.
10094-8{_}3663{_}Then I lighted my pipe and sat down in a chair opposite the sleeper, and waited for him to awake.
10094-8{_}3664{_}I had not long to wait.
10094-8{_}3665{_}Whether from my eyes on his face, or some other cause, he stirred uneasily, opened his eyes, and sat suddenly bolt upright.
10094-8{_}3666{_}"Why, Tom," he cried, as he saw me, "I must have been asleep."
10094-8{_}3667{_}"So you have," I said, shaking hands with him, and pressing him back into the chair, from which he would have risen.
10094-8{_}3668{_}"But what fortunate chance has brought you here?"
10094-8{_}3669{_}"The most fortunate in the world!"
10094-8{_}3670{_}he cried, his eyes agleam.
10094-8{_}3671{_}"You know I told you that the governor and House of Burgesses would not bear quietly the project to leave our frontier open to the enemy.
10094-8{_}3672{_}Well, read this," and he drew from his pocket a most formidable looking paper.
10094-8{_}3673{_}I took it with a trembling hand and carried it to the window, but the moon was almost set, and I could not decipher it.
10094-8{_}3674{_}"What is it?"
10094-8{_}3675{_}I asked, quivering with impatience.
10094-8{_}3676{_}"Here, give it to me," he said, with a light laugh, which reminded me of the night I had seen him first in the governor's palace at Williamsburg.
10094-8{_}3677{_}"The House of Burgesses has just met.
10094-8{_}3678{_}They ordered that a regiment of a thousand men be raised to protect the frontier in addition to those already in the field, and voted 20,000 for the defense of the colony."
10094-8{_}3679{_}"And that is your commission!"
10094-8{_}3680{_}I cried.
10094-8{_}3681{_}"Is it not so?"
10094-8{_}3682{_}"Yes," he said, scarce less excited than myself.
10094-8{_}3683{_}"'Tis my commission as commander-in-chief of all the Virginia forces."
10094-8{_}3684{_}I wrung his hand with joy unutterable.
10094-8{_}3685{_}At last this man, who had done so much, was to know something beside disappointment and discouragement.
10094-8{_}3686{_}"But you do not ask how you are concerned in all this," he continued, smiling into my face, "or why I rode over myself to bring the news to you.
10094-8{_}3687{_}'Tis because I set out to-morrow at daybreak for Winchester to take command, and I wish you to go with me, Tom, as aide-de-camp, with the rank of captain."
10094-8{_}3688{_}CHAPTER XXIV  A WARNING FROM THE FOREST   It was at Winchester that Colonel Washington established his headquarters, maintaining a detachment at Fort Cumberland sufficient to repel any attack the Indians were like to make against it, and to cut off such of their war parties as ventured east of it.
10094-8{_}3689{_}From Winchester he was able more easily to keep in touch with all parts of the frontier, and with the string of blockhouses which had been built years before as a gathering-place for the settlers in the event of Indian incursions.
10094-8{_}3690{_}By the first of September his arrangements had been completed, but long before that time it was evident the task was to be no easy one.
10094-8{_}3691{_}Already, from the high passes of the Alleghenies, war parties of Delawares and Shawanoes had descended, sweeping down upon the frontier families like a devastating whirlwind, and butchering men, women, and children with impartial fury.
10094-8{_}3692{_}The unbounded forest, which covered hill and valley with a curtain of unbroken foliage, afforded a thousand lurking-places, and it was well-nigh impossible for an armed force to get within striking distance of the marauders.
10094-8{_}3693{_}So, almost daily, stories of horrible cruelty came to the fort, plunging the commander into an agony of rage and dejection at his very impotence.
10094-8{_}3694{_}The fort was soon crowded with refugees,--wives bewailing their husbands, husbands swearing to avenge their wives, parents lamenting their children, children of a sudden made orphans,--and from north and south, scores of hard-featured, steel-eyed men came to us, their rifles in their hands, to offer their services, and after a time these came to be one of the most valuable portions of our force.
10094-8{_}3695{_}Ah, the stories they told us!
10094-8{_}3696{_}Tragedies such as that which Spiltdorph and I had come upon had been repeated scores of times.
10094-8{_}3697{_}The settler who had left his cabin at daybreak in search of game, or to carry his furs to the nearest post, returned at sundown to find only a smoking heap of ashes where his home had been, and among them the charred and mutilated bodies of his wife and children.
10094-8{_}3698{_}Horror succeeded horror, and the climax came one day when we were passing a little schoolhouse some miles below the fort, in the midst of a district well populated.
10094-8{_}3699{_}Wondering at the unwonted silence, we dismounted, opened the door, and looked within.
10094-8{_}3700{_}The master lay upon the platform with his pupils around him, all dead and newly scalped.
10094-8{_}3701{_}The savages had passed that way not half an hour before.
10094-8{_}3702{_}And to add to the trials of the commander, his troops, hastily got together, were most of them impatient of restraint or discipline, and with no knowledge of warfare, while the governor and the House of Burgesses demanded that he undertake impossibilities.
10094-8{_}3703{_}It was a dreary, trying, thankless task.
10094-8{_}3704{_}"They expect me to perform miracles," he said to me bitterly one day.
10094-8{_}3705{_}"How am I to protect a frontier four hundred miles in length with five or six hundred effective men, against an enemy who knows every foot of the ground, and who can find a hiding-place at every step?"
10094-8{_}3706{_}Only by the sternest measures could many of the levies be brought to the fort, and one man--a captain, God save the mark!--sent word that he and his company could not come because their corn had not yet been got in.
10094-8{_}3707{_}Yet, in spite of all these drawbacks, we did accomplish something.
10094-8{_}3708{_}There were a few of the Iroquois who yet remained our friends, and the general spared no effort to retain their goodwill, for their services were invaluable.
10094-8{_}3709{_}With a lofty contempt for the Delawares and Shawanoes, whom they had one time subjugated and compelled to assume the name of women, they roamed the forest for miles around, and more than once enabled us to ambush one of the war parties and send it howling back to the Muskingum, where there was great weeping and wailing in the lodges upon its return.
10094-8{_}3710{_}But it was fruitless work, for the Indians, driven back for the moment, returned with augmented fury, and again drenched the frontier in the blood of the colonists.
10094-8{_}3711{_}We realized one and all that nothing we could do would turn the tide of war permanently from our borders and render the frontier safe until the French had been driven from Fort Duquesne.
10094-8{_}3712{_}For it was they who urged the Indians on, supplying them with guns and ammunition, and rewarding them with rum when they returned to the fort laden with English scalps.
10094-8{_}3713{_}An expedition against the French stronghold was for the present out of the question, and we could only bite our nails and curse, waiting for another night when we might sally forth and fall upon one of the war parties.
10094-8{_}3714{_}But the few Indians we killed seemed a pitiful atonement for the mangled bodies scattered along the frontier and the hundreds of homes of which there remained nothing but blackened ruins.
10094-8{_}3715{_}As the weeks passed and the Indians saw our impotence, they grew bolder, slipped through the chain of blockhouses, and ravaged the country east of us, disappearing into the woods as if by magic at the first alarm.
10094-8{_}3716{_}The month of August and the first portion of September wore away in this dreary manner, and it was perhaps a week later that Colonel Washington sent me to Frederick to make arrangements for some supplies.
10094-8{_}3717{_}The distance, which was a scant fifty miles, was over a well-traveled road, and through a district so well protected that the Indians had not dared to visit it; so I rode out of the fort one morning, taking with me only my negro boy Sam, whom I had selected for my servant since the day he had warned me against Polete.
10094-8{_}3718{_}I remember that the day was very warm, and that there was no air stirring, so that we pushed forward with indifferent speed.
10094-8{_}3719{_}At noon we reached a farmhouse owned by John Evans, where we remained until the heat had somewhat moderated, and set forward again about four o'clock in the afternoon.
10094-8{_}3720{_}We had ridden for near an hour, and I was deep in my own thoughts, when I heard something breaking its way through the underbrush, and the next moment my horse shied violently as a negro stumbled blindly into the road and collapsed into a heap before he had taken half a dozen steps along it.
10094-8{_}3721{_}I reined up sharply, and as I did so, heard Sam give a shrill cry of alarm.
10094-8{_}3722{_}"Shut up, boy," I cried, "and get off and see what ails the man.
10094-8{_}3723{_}He can't hurt you."
10094-8{_}3724{_}But Sam sat in his saddle clutching at his horse's neck, his face spotted with terror as I had seen it once before.
10094-8{_}3725{_}"What is it, Sam?"
10094-8{_}3726{_}I asked impatiently.
10094-8{_}3727{_}"Good Gawd, Mas' Tom," he cried, his teeth chattering together and cutting off his words queerly, "don' yo' see who 'tis?
10094-8{_}3728{_}Don' yo' know him?"
10094-8{_}3729{_}"Know him?
10094-8{_}3730{_}No, of course not," I answered sharply.
10094-8{_}3731{_}"Who is he?"
10094-8{_}3732{_}"Polete," gasped Sam.
10094-8{_}3733{_}"Polete, come back aftah me," and seemed incapable of another word.
10094-8{_}3734{_}In an instant I was off my horse and kneeling in the road beside the fallen man.
10094-8{_}3735{_}Not till then did I believe it was Polete.
10094-8{_}3736{_}From a great gash in the side of his head the blood had soaked into his hair and dried over his face.
10094-8{_}3737{_}His shirt was stained, apparently from a wound in his breast, but most horrible of all was a circular, reeking spot on the crown of his head from which the scalp had been stripped.
10094-8{_}3738{_}It needed no second glance to tell me that Polete had been in the hands of the Indians.
10094-8{_}3739{_}By this time Sam had partially recovered his wits, and being convinced that it was Polete in the flesh, not in the spirit, brought some water from a spring at the roadside.
10094-8{_}3740{_}I bathed Polete's head as well as I could, and washed the blood from his face.
10094-8{_}3741{_}Tearing open his shirt, I saw that blood was slowly welling from an ugly wound in his breast.
10094-8{_}3742{_}He opened his eyes after a moment, and stared vacantly up into my face.
10094-8{_}3743{_}"Debbils," he moaned, "debbils, t' kill a po' ole man.
10094-8{_}3744{_}Ain't I said I done gwine t' lib wid yo'?
10094-8{_}3745{_}Kain't trabble fas' 'nough fo' yo'?
10094-8{_}3746{_}Don' shoot, oh, don' shoot!
10094-8{_}3747{_}Ah!"
10094-8{_}3748{_}He dropped back again into the road with a groan, and tossed from side to side.
10094-8{_}3749{_}I thought he was dying, but when I dashed more water in his face, he opened his eyes again.
10094-8{_}3750{_}This time he seemed to know me.
10094-8{_}3751{_}"Is it Mas' Tom?"
10094-8{_}3752{_}he gasped.
10094-8{_}3753{_}"Mas' Tom what let me go?"
10094-8{_}3754{_}"Yes, Polete," I answered gently, "it's Master Tom."
10094-8{_}3755{_}"Whar am I?"
10094-8{_}3756{_}he asked faintly.
10094-8{_}3757{_}"Have dee got me 'gin?
10094-8{_}3758{_}Dee gwine to buhn me?"
10094-8{_}3759{_}"No, no," I said.
10094-8{_}3760{_}"Nobody 's going to harm you, Polete.
10094-8{_}3761{_}Where have you been all this time?"
10094-8{_}3762{_}"In d' woods," he whispered, "hidin' in d' swamps, an' skulkin' long aftah night.
10094-8{_}3763{_}Could n' nevah sleep, Mas' Tom.
10094-8{_}3764{_}When I went t' sleep, seemed laike d' dogs was right aftah me."
10094-8{_}3765{_}His head fell back again, and a rush of blood in his throat almost choked him.
10094-8{_}3766{_}"Wish I'd stayed at d' plantation, Mas' Tom," he whispered.
10094-8{_}3767{_}"Nothin' could n' been no wo'se 'n what I went frough.
10094-8{_}3768{_}Kep' 'long d' ribbah, laike yo' said, but could n' git nothin' t' eat only berries growin' in d' woods.
10094-8{_}3769{_}Got mighty weak, 'n' den las' night met d' Injuns."
10094-8{_}3770{_}"Last night!"
10094-8{_}3771{_}I cried.
10094-8{_}3772{_}"Where, Polete?"
10094-8{_}3773{_}"Obah dah 'long d' ribbah," he answered faintly.
10094-8{_}3774{_}"Dee gib me some'n' t' eat, an' I frought maybe dee'd take me 'long, but dis mornin' dee had a big powwow, an' dee shot me an' knock me in d' haid.
10094-8{_}3775{_}Seems laike dee 's gwine t' buhn a big plantation t'-night."
10094-8{_}3776{_}"A big plantation, Polete?"
10094-8{_}3777{_}I asked.
10094-8{_}3778{_}"Where?
10094-8{_}3779{_}Tell me--oh, you must tell me!"
10094-8{_}3780{_}But his head had fallen back, and his eyes were closed.
10094-8{_}3781{_}There was another burst of blood from his nose and mouth.
10094-8{_}3782{_}I threw water over his face, slapped his hands, and shouted into his ears, but to no avail.
10094-8{_}3783{_}Sam brought me another hatful of water, but his hands trembled so that when he set it down, he spilled half of it.
10094-8{_}3784{_}I dashed what was left over the dying man, but his breathing grew slow and slower, and still his eyes were closed.
10094-8{_}3785{_}I trembled to think what would happen should I never learn where the Indians were going, if Polete should never open his eyes again to tell me.
10094-8{_}3786{_}But he did, at last,--oh, how long it seemed!--he did, and gazed up at me with a little smile.
10094-8{_}3787{_}"Reckon it's all obah wid ole Polete, Mas' Tom," he whispered.
10094-8{_}3788{_}"Where is this plantation, Polete?"
10094-8{_}3789{_}I asked.
10094-8{_}3790{_}"The plantation the Indians are going to attack.
10094-8{_}3791{_}Quick, tell me."
10094-8{_}3792{_}He looked at me a moment longer before answering.
10094-8{_}3793{_}"D' plantation?
10094-8{_}3794{_}Obah dah, eight, ten mile, neah d' ribbah," and he made a faint little motion northward with his hand.
10094-8{_}3795{_}The motion, slight as it was, brought on another hemorrhage.
10094-8{_}3796{_}His eyes looked up into mine for a moment longer, and then, even as I gazed at them, grew fixed and glazed.
10094-8{_}3797{_}Old Polete was dead.
10094-8{_}3798{_}We laid him by the side of the road and rolled two or three logs over him.
10094-8{_}3799{_}More we could not do, for every moment was precious.
10094-8{_}3800{_}"Sam," I said quickly, as we finished our task, "you must ride to the fort as fast as your horse will carry you.
10094-8{_}3801{_}Tell Colonel Washington that I sent you, and that the Indians are going to attack some big plantation on the river eight or ten miles north of here.
10094-8{_}3802{_}Tell him that I have gone on to warn them.
10094-8{_}3803{_}Do you understand?"
10094-8{_}3804{_}"Yes, sah," he gasped.
10094-8{_}3805{_}"Well, don't you forget a word of it," I said sternly.
10094-8{_}3806{_}"You can reach the fort easily by nine o'clock to-night.
10094-8{_}3807{_}Now, be off."
10094-8{_}3808{_}He hesitated a moment.
10094-8{_}3809{_}"What is it?"
10094-8{_}3810{_}I cried.
10094-8{_}3811{_}"You are not afraid, boy?"
10094-8{_}3812{_}He rubbed his eyes and began to whimper.
10094-8{_}3813{_}"Not fo' myself, Mas' Tom," he said.
10094-8{_}3814{_}"But yo' gwine t' ride right into d' Injuns.
10094-8{_}3815{_}Dee'll git yo' suah."
10094-8{_}3816{_}"Nonsense!"
10094-8{_}3817{_}I retorted sharply.
10094-8{_}3818{_}"I'll get through all right, and we can easily hold out till reinforcements come.
10094-8{_}3819{_}Now get on your horse.
10094-8{_}3820{_}Remember, the faster you go, the surer you'll be to save us all."
10094-8{_}3821{_}He swung himself into the saddle, and turned for a moment to look at me, the tears streaming down his face.
10094-8{_}3822{_}He seemed to think me as good as dead already.
10094-8{_}3823{_}"Good-by, Sam," I said.
10094-8{_}3824{_}"Good-by, Mas' Tom," and he put spurs to his horse and set off down the road.
10094-8{_}3825{_}I watched him until the trees hid him from sight, and then sprang upon my horse and started forward.
10094-8{_}3826{_}Eight or ten miles, Polete had said, northward near the river.
10094-8{_}3827{_}The road served me for some miles, and then I came to a cross road, which seemed well traveled.
10094-8{_}3828{_}Not doubting that this led to the plantation of which I was in search, I turned into it, and proceeded onward as rapidly as the darkness of the woods permitted.
10094-8{_}3829{_}Evening was at hand, and under the overlapping branches of the trees, the gloom grew deep and deeper.
10094-8{_}3830{_}At last, away to the right, I caught the gleam of water, and with a sigh of relief knew I was near the river and so on the right road.
10094-8{_}3831{_}The house could not be much farther on.
10094-8{_}3832{_}With renewed vigor I urged my horse forward, and in a few minutes came to the edge of a clearing, and there before me was the house.
10094-8{_}3833{_}But it was not this which drew my eyes.
10094-8{_}3834{_}Far away on the other side, concealed from the house by a grove of trees, a shadowy line of tiny figures was emerging from the forest.
10094-8{_}3835{_}Even as I looked, they vanished, and I rubbed my eyes in bewilderment.
10094-8{_}3836{_}Yet I knew they had not deceived me.
10094-8{_}3837{_}It was the war party preparing for the attack.
10094-8{_}3838{_}I set spurs to my horse and galloped the jaded beast toward the house as fast as his weary legs would carry him.
10094-8{_}3839{_}As I drew near, I saw it was a large and well-built mansion.
10094-8{_}3840{_}Lights gleamed through the open doors and windows.
10094-8{_}3841{_}Evidently none there dreamed of danger, and I thanked God that I should be in time.
10094-8{_}3842{_}In a moment I was at the door, and as I threw myself from the saddle, I heard from the open window a ringing laugh which thrilled me through and through, for I knew that the voice was Dorothy's.
10094-8{_}3843{_}CHAPTER XXV  I FIND MYSELF IN A DELICATE SITUATION   I staggered up the steps, reeling as from a blow on the head, and a negro met me at the top.
10094-8{_}3844{_}"Where is your master?"
10094-8{_}3845{_}I asked.
10094-8{_}3846{_}"Kun'l Ma'sh 's obah at Frederick, sah," he answered, looking at me with astonished eyes.
10094-8{_}3847{_}"Your mistress, then, quick, boy!"
10094-8{_}3848{_}and as he turned toward the open door with a gesture of his hand, I hurried after him.
10094-8{_}3849{_}There was a buzz of conversation in the room as we approached, but it ceased abruptly as we entered.
10094-8{_}3850{_}I felt rather than saw that Dorothy was there, but I looked only at the plump little woman who half rose from her chair and stared at me in astonishment.
10094-8{_}3851{_}I suppose my appearance was sufficiently surprising, but there was no time to think of that.
10094-8{_}3852{_}"A gen'leman t' see yo', Mis' Ma'sh," said my guide.
10094-8{_}3853{_}I had not caught the name before, but now I understood, and as I looked at the woman before me, I saw her likeness to her son.
10094-8{_}3854{_}"I am Captain Stewart, Mrs. Marsh," I said, controlling my voice as well as I could.
10094-8{_}3855{_}"You may, perhaps, have heard of me.
10094-8{_}3856{_}If not, there are others present who can vouch for me," but I did not move my eyes from her face.
10094-8{_}3857{_}"That is quite unnecessary, Captain Stewart," she cried, coming to me and giving me her hand very prettily.
10094-8{_}3858{_}"I knew your grandfather, and you resemble him greatly."
10094-8{_}3859{_}And then she stopped suddenly and grew very pale.
10094-8{_}3860{_}"I remember now," she said.
10094-8{_}3861{_}"You were in dear Harry's company."
10094-8{_}3862{_}"I was not in his company, but I knew and loved him well," I answered gently, taking both her hands and holding them tight in mine.
10094-8{_}3863{_}"He was a brave and gallant boy, and lost his life while trying to save another's.
10094-8{_}3864{_}I was with him when he fell."
10094-8{_}3865{_}She came close to me, and I could feel that she was trembling.
10094-8{_}3866{_}"And did he suffer?"
10094-8{_}3867{_}she asked.
10094-8{_}3868{_}"Oh, I cannot bear to think that he should suffer!"
10094-8{_}3869{_}"He did not suffer," I said.
10094-8{_}3870{_}"He was shot through the heart.
10094-8{_}3871{_}He did not have an instant's pain."
10094-8{_}3872{_}She was crying softly against my shoulder, but I held her from me.
10094-8{_}3873{_}"Mrs. Marsh," I said, "it is not of Harry we must think now, but of ourselves.
10094-8{_}3874{_}This afternoon I learned that the Indians had planned an attack upon this place to-night.
10094-8{_}3875{_}I sent my servant back to the fort for reinforcements and rode on to give the alarm.
10094-8{_}3876{_}As I neared the house, I saw their war party skulking in the woods, so that the attack may not be long delayed."
10094-8{_}3877{_}Her face had turned ashen, and I was glad that I had kept her hands in mine, else she would have fallen.
10094-8{_}3878{_}"There is no danger," I added cheerily.
10094-8{_}3879{_}"We must close the doors and windows, and we can easily keep them off till morning.
10094-8{_}3880{_}The troops will be here by that time."
10094-8{_}3881{_}"Oh, do you think so?"
10094-8{_}3882{_}she gasped.
10094-8{_}3883{_}"I am sure of it.
10094-8{_}3884{_}Now, will you give the orders to the servants?"
10094-8{_}3885{_}But that was not necessary.
10094-8{_}3886{_}The man who had shown me in had heard my words, and already had the other servants at work, closing and barring doors and windows.
10094-8{_}3887{_}I saw that my assistance was not needed.
10094-8{_}3888{_}Then for the first time I looked at Dorothy.
10094-8{_}3889{_}She was standing, leaning lightly with one hand upon a table, her eyes large and dark with terror, and her lips quivering, perhaps at the scene which had gone before.
10094-8{_}3890{_}Her mother was seated by her, and it was to her I turned.
10094-8{_}3891{_}"I beg you to believe, Mrs. Stewart," I said, "that I did not know you and your daughter were here.
10094-8{_}3892{_}Indeed, I thought you both were back at Riverview ere this."
10094-8{_}3893{_}"I believe you, Mr. Stewart," she answered softly.
10094-8{_}3894{_}"I believe you to be a man of honor.
10094-8{_}3895{_}I am sure I can trust you."
10094-8{_}3896{_}There was a tone in her voice which I had never heard before.
10094-8{_}3897{_}"Thank you," I said.
10094-8{_}3898{_}"I shall try to deserve your trust," and then I turned away to look to our defenses.
10094-8{_}3899{_}I confess that, after the first five minutes, our situation appeared more perilous than I had at first believed it.
10094-8{_}3900{_}There was no white man in the house except myself, only a dozen negro servants, five of whom were men.
10094-8{_}3901{_}A boy, whom I sent to the negro quarters to bring reinforcements, returned with the news that they were deserted, but he brought back with him the overseer, a man named Brightson, who was to prove his mettle before the night was out.
10094-8{_}3902{_}"I suspected this afternoon that there was something in the wind," he said to me, when I had explained our situation, "though I could not guess what it was.
10094-8{_}3903{_}The niggers were so damned quiet, not singing in the field as they always do.
10094-8{_}3904{_}They've been mighty uneasy for a month back."
10094-8{_}3905{_}"Yes, I know," I interrupted.
10094-8{_}3906{_}"It's the same all over the colony.
10094-8{_}3907{_}They think the French are going to help them kill the English.
10094-8{_}3908{_}I'm rather glad they ran away.
10094-8{_}3909{_}How about these house niggers?"
10094-8{_}3910{_}"Oh, they're all right, especially Pomp there.
10094-8{_}3911{_}They'll help us all they can."
10094-8{_}3912{_}"That makes seven of us, then.
10094-8{_}3913{_}Can you shoot?"
10094-8{_}3914{_}"Try me," he answered simply.
10094-8{_}3915{_}"All right," I said.
10094-8{_}3916{_}"We'll pull through, I think.
10094-8{_}3917{_}Indians are no good at anything but a surprise.
10094-8{_}3918{_}I dare say some of the niggers have told them that there would be no men here to-night, so they think they'll have an easy victory."
10094-8{_}3919{_}I had ordered Pomp to bring to the hall all the arms and ammunition in the house, and at this moment he touched me on the elbow and told me this was done.
10094-8{_}3920{_}Brightson and I looked over the collection, and found it as complete as could be desired.
10094-8{_}3921{_}There were a dozen muskets, half a dozen pairs of pistols, a pile of swords and hangers, and ammunition in plenty.
10094-8{_}3922{_}Evidently, Colonel Marsh had foreseen the possibility of an Indian attack, and was prepared to receive it.
10094-8{_}3923{_}A tour of the house showed me, moreover, that it had been built with the same possibility in view.
10094-8{_}3924{_}The doors and shutters were all strong and double-barred, and moreover were loopholed in a way that enabled us to command both approaches.
10094-8{_}3925{_}I divided the arms, and posted Brightson with three men at the rear door, while I, with Pomp and another negro, took a place at the front.
10094-8{_}3926{_}The women I sent to the top of the staircase, where they would be out of reach of any flying bullets, and could at the same time see what was going on.
10094-8{_}3927{_}It was my aunt who protested against this arrangement.
10094-8{_}3928{_}"Can we not be of use, Captain Stewart?"
10094-8{_}3929{_}she asked.
10094-8{_}3930{_}"We could at least load the muskets for you."
10094-8{_}3931{_}"And I am sure that I could fire one," cried Dorothy.
10094-8{_}3932{_}"No, no," I laughed.
10094-8{_}3933{_}"Time enough for that when there is need.
10094-8{_}3934{_}They will not fancy the reception they will get, and may not return for a second dose."
10094-8{_}3935{_}And with a sudden tenderness at my heart, right under the eyes of Mrs. Stewart, I reached up, caught Dorothy's hand, and kissed it.
10094-8{_}3936{_}When I glanced up again, I saw that she was smiling down at me, but I dared not look at her mother's face.
10094-8{_}3937{_}I had wondered at first why the attack was not made at once, but as I stood looking out at my loophole, I perceived the reason.
10094-8{_}3938{_}The first shade of evening had found the moon high in the heavens, and it was now rapidly sinking toward the line of trees which marked the horizon.
10094-8{_}3939{_}Once plunged behind them, the darkness would enable the Indians to creep up to the house unseen.
10094-8{_}3940{_}I watched the moon as it dropped slowly down the sky.
10094-8{_}3941{_}The lower rim just touched the treetops--then it was half behind them--then it had disappeared, and the world was plunged in darkness.
10094-8{_}3942{_}I peered into the gloom with starting eyes, but could see nothing.
10094-8{_}3943{_}I strained my ears, but could catch no sound; three or four tense minutes passed, I could have sworn it was half an hour.
10094-8{_}3944{_}One of the negro women on the stair screamed slightly, and, as though it were a signal, there came a great blow upon the door and pandemonium arose without.
10094-8{_}3945{_}I fired blindly through my loophole, seized the musket at my side, and fired a second time, then emptied both my pistols out into the night.
10094-8{_}3946{_}It seemed to me a hundred rifles were being fired at once.
10094-8{_}3947{_}The hall was full of smoke and the pungent smell of powder, and then, in a second, all was still.
10094-8{_}3948{_}But only for a second.
10094-8{_}3949{_}For there came another chorus of yells from a distance, and I could hear the negro women on the steps behind me wailing softly.
10094-8{_}3950{_}"Load!"
10094-8{_}3951{_}I shouted.
10094-8{_}3952{_}"Load, Pomp!
10094-8{_}3953{_}They will be back in a minute," and then I ran to the other door to see how Brightson fared.
10094-8{_}3954{_}"All right," he said cheerfully, in answer to my question.
10094-8{_}3955{_}"We couldn't see 'em, but we emptied a good deal of lead out there, and I think from the way they yelled we must have hit two or three."
10094-8{_}3956{_}"Keep it up!"
10094-8{_}3957{_}I cried.
10094-8{_}3958{_}"We'll drive them off easily," and with a word of encouragement to the negroes, I returned to my post.
10094-8{_}3959{_}As I neared the door, I saw two figures in white working over the guns.
10094-8{_}3960{_}It was Dorothy and her mother, helping the negroes reload.
10094-8{_}3961{_}I sent them back to the stair with affected sternness, but I got a second hand-clasp from Dorothy as she passed me.
10094-8{_}3962{_}Then came another long period of waiting, which racked the nerves until the silence grew well-nigh insupportable.
10094-8{_}3963{_}The darkness without was absolute, and there was not a sound to disturb the stillness.
10094-8{_}3964{_}The minutes passed, and I was just beginning to hope that the Indians had already got enough, when I caught the faint shuffle of moccasined feet on the porch, and again the door was struck a terrific blow, which made it groan on its hinges.
10094-8{_}3965{_}I fired out into the darkness as fast as I could lay down one gun and pick up another, and again the uproar ceased as suddenly as it had begun.
10094-8{_}3966{_}As I turned away a moment from the loophole, I saw that Pomp had sunk down to the floor, his hands to his head.
10094-8{_}3967{_}"What is it, Pomp?"
10094-8{_}3968{_}I cried, as I bent over him, but there was no need for him to answer, even had he been able.
10094-8{_}3969{_}A bullet, entering the loop-hole through which he was firing, had struck his left eye and entered the head.
10094-8{_}3970{_}The other negro and myself laid him to one side against the wall, and when I went to him ten minutes later to see if there was anything I could do, he was dead.
10094-8{_}3971{_}I turned away to the women to say some words of cheer and comfort to them, when a call from Brightson startled me.
10094-8{_}3972{_}"What has happened?"
10094-8{_}3973{_}I asked, as I reached his side, and for answer he pointed out through the loophole.
10094-8{_}3974{_}"They have fired the nigger quarters and outbuildings," he said grimly.
10094-8{_}3975{_}"They'll probably try to fire the house next."
10094-8{_}3976{_}Even as we looked, the flames rose high above the roofs of the cabins and bathed the clearing in red radiance.
10094-8{_}3977{_}In and out among the buildings we could see the Indians scampering, a hundred of them at least.
10094-8{_}3978{_}Suddenly there was a chorus of yells, and two Indians appeared, rolling a cask before them into the belt of light.
10094-8{_}3979{_}"They've found a keg of rum which was in my quarters," remarked Brightson; "now they'll get crazy drunk.
10094-8{_}3980{_}Our task has just begun, Captain Stewart."
10094-8{_}3981{_}I realized that he spoke the truth.
10094-8{_}3982{_}Sober, an Indian will not stand up long in open fight, but drunk, he is a devil incarnate,--a fiend who will dare anything.
10094-8{_}3983{_}I watched them as they knocked in the head of the cask and scooped up the raw spirits within.
10094-8{_}3984{_}Then one of them began a melancholy melody, which rose and fell in measured cadence, the other warriors gradually joining in and stamping the ground with their feet.
10094-8{_}3985{_}Every minute one would run to the cask for another draught of the rum, and gradually their yells grew louder, their excitement more intense, as they rushed back and forth brandishing their weapons.
10094-8{_}3986{_}"They will soon be on us again," said Brightson in a low tone, but round and round they kept dancing, their leader in front in all his war trappings, the others almost naked, and for the most part painted black.
10094-8{_}3987{_}No wonder I had been unable to see them in the darkness.
10094-8{_}3988{_}"They are going to attack us again, Tom, are they not?"
10094-8{_}3989{_}asked a low voice at my elbow.
10094-8{_}3990{_}"Dorothy," I cried, "what are you doing here?
10094-8{_}3991{_}Come, you must get back to the stair at once.
10094-8{_}3992{_}The attack may come at any moment."
10094-8{_}3993{_}"You are treating me like a child," she protested, and her eyes flashed passionately.
10094-8{_}3994{_}"Do you think we are cowards, we women?
10094-8{_}3995{_}We will not be treated so!
10094-8{_}3996{_}We have come to help you."
10094-8{_}3997{_}I looked at her in amazement.
10094-8{_}3998{_}This was not the Dorothy I knew, but a braver, sweeter one.
10094-8{_}3999{_}Her mother and Mrs. Marsh were behind her, both looking equally determined.
10094-8{_}4000{_}"Very well," I said, yielding with an ill grace.
10094-8{_}4001{_}"You may sit on the floor here and load the guns as we fire them.
10094-8{_}4002{_}That will be of greater service than if you fired them yourselves, and you will be quite out of reach of the bullets."
10094-8{_}4003{_}Dorothy sniffed contemptuously at my last words, but deigned to sit down beside the other women.
10094-8{_}4004{_}I placed the powder and ball where they could reach them easily, shaded a candle so that it threw its light only on the floor beside them, gave them a few directions about loading, and rejoined Brightson at his loophole.
10094-8{_}4005{_}The Indians had stopped dancing, and were engaged in heaping up a great pile of burning logs.
10094-8{_}4006{_}"What are they about?"
10094-8{_}4007{_}I asked.
10094-8{_}4008{_}Brightson looked at me with a grim light in his eyes.
10094-8{_}4009{_}"They're going to try to burn us out," he said, and almost before he had spoken, the Indians seized a hundred burning brands from the fire, and waving them about their heads to fan them to a brighter flame, started toward us.
10094-8{_}4010{_}CHAPTER XXVI  A DESPERATE DEFENSE   I had barely time to get back to my post at the front door when they were upon us.
10094-8{_}4011{_}I fired out into the rabble, and as I turned to get another gun, Dorothy was at my side and thrust it into my hands.
10094-8{_}4012{_}There was no time to protest, even had I not realized, as I glanced into her eyes, that protestation would be useless.
10094-8{_}4013{_}I fired a second time, when a tremendous explosion in the hall at my side startled me.
10094-8{_}4014{_}I saw in a moment what had happened.
10094-8{_}4015{_}The negro who was at the other loophole, dazed with fear, had discharged his gun straight into the ceiling overhead, and then, flinging it down, turned and ran.
10094-8{_}4016{_}I could not pursue him, and grabbing a third gun from Dorothy, I fired again at the Indians, some of whom were swarming up the steps.
10094-8{_}4017{_}As I did so, I stared an instant in amazement, for at the shot two men had fallen.
10094-8{_}4018{_}As I turned back for another musket, I saw Mrs. Stewart at the other loophole, a smoking rifle in her hands, into which she was feverishly ramming another charge.
10094-8{_}4019{_}It was a sight that made my heart leap, and I found myself suddenly admiring her.
10094-8{_}4020{_}But before either of us could fire again, the Indians were gone, and a chorus of yells and sharp firing told me they were attacking Brightson's side of the house.
10094-8{_}4021{_}The noise died away after a moment, and they appeared again borne distance off, looking back eagerly as though expecting something.
10094-8{_}4022{_}I saw with a start that their firebrands were no longer in their hands, and a moment later a puff of smoke from the corner of the house and the exultant yells of the savages warned me of our new danger.
10094-8{_}4023{_}As I turned from the door, I met Brightson coming to seek me with an anxious face.
10094-8{_}4024{_}"They have fired the house, Captain Stewart," he said.
10094-8{_}4025{_}"I fear so.
10094-8{_}4026{_}We must find the place and put out the flames."
10094-8{_}4027{_}Without a word he turned and followed me, and we opened the shutters a little here and there and looked out.
10094-8{_}4028{_}We soon found what we were seeking.
10094-8{_}4029{_}As the Indians had dashed around the house from front to rear, they had approached the side and piled their burning brands against the boards.
10094-8{_}4030{_}We looked down from the window and saw that the house had already caught fire.
10094-8{_}4031{_}In a few moments the flames would be beyond control.
10094-8{_}4032{_}I was back to the hall in an instant.
10094-8{_}4033{_}"Is there any water in the house?"
10094-8{_}4034{_}I asked of Mrs. Marsh, who was seated on the floor reloading our guns with a coolness which told me where her son had got his gallantry.
10094-8{_}4035{_}She looked at me an instant with face whitened by a new fear.
10094-8{_}4036{_}"Do you mean that the house is on fire?"
10094-8{_}4037{_}she asked.
10094-8{_}4038{_}I nodded.
10094-8{_}4039{_}"There is no water," she said very quietly.
10094-8{_}4040{_}"The well is a hundred yards from the house."
10094-8{_}4041{_}I beckoned to the negroes, who were listening in an anxious group, and hastened back to Brightson.
10094-8{_}4042{_}"There is no water," I said to him briefly.
10094-8{_}4043{_}"I am going to open the shutter, drop down, and knock the fire away from the house.
10094-8{_}4044{_}Do you be ready to pull me back in again, when I have finished."
10094-8{_}4045{_}"But it is death to do that," he exclaimed.
10094-8{_}4046{_}"No, no," I said.
10094-8{_}4047{_}"You and the boys can keep them off.
10094-8{_}4048{_}There is no other way."
10094-8{_}4049{_}He turned from me and looked about the room.
10094-8{_}4050{_}"This will save you," he cried, and ran to a heavy oak table which stood in one corner.
